What (Issue): Decisively from one aspect: The form of command attached to a condition or description is said to be put for repetition with repetition of the condition and description absolutely, whether the condition or description is a cause or not. And it is said: The attached command is not for repetition absolutely. If it is a cause, then does the command repeat with its repetition by reason? There is disagreement, and the truth is yes, it repeats (and it is said no, it does not). When disagreement is established on this pattern, the claim of consensus on the cause, as in al-Mukhtasar and others, that it repeats with its repetition is wrong. And it is not correct to accuse the claimant of consensus of error, that the Hanafis say it does not repeat with repetition of the condition even if it is a cause, because their intent is that it does not indicate by the wording, rather the indication is only rational. Yes, after establishing the existence of disagreement as the author narrated, consensus is negated, but it is very far to deny the ruling after establishing causality except from those who reject analogy absolutely. To us: First: it preceded that the form (hay'a) is only for the demand, and the matter is for the reality as such, so there is no repetition as preceded. Second: if he entered the market, buy such and such, it does not repeat, otherwise it would be like 'whenever' (kullama), and it is not understood from it repetition, for it is agreed that it is not like 'kullama'. As for repetition by the cause attached to it, it is due to the necessity of repeating the effect with its repetition, because non-occurrence is impossible. So the intention is that it only repeats when obstacles are removed, and then non-occurrence is absolutely impossible. Then this restriction is only for those who allow specification of the cause by an impediment. And this repetition is not by the wording but by reason. And in al-Hashiya: not by consensus as Ibn al-Hajib claimed. End. That is because consensus has not been established; rather, disagreement is established. And if it is said: The intent of those who say it is by the wording is that attachment to description and condition is suggestive of causality linguistically, and the effect repeats with repetition of the cause, but since this indication is speculative, it may fall short of the indicator. I say: Then the dispute becomes verbal. For the intent of the majority who negate repetition by wording is that it is not put for it. So understand. If you say: Why then does divorce not repeat with repetition of entry in the attachment to it for reason of causality? He said: Divorce does not repeat with the entry attached to it due to the non-consideration of its being a cause for divorce. If you say: If the attached thing is a cause, repetition must happen with its repetition; why then do the Hanafis not cut off the thief's hand on the third theft if the theft is the cause for cutting? And they flog in zina forever as long as he commits zina after flogging, even though both are causes. We say: Theft is a cause for cutting one hand only, because two hands are not cut for one theft by consensus. And it is supported that plural addressing plural requires distribution of units to units. The meaning: Cut off the hand of the male thief and the hand of the female thief; the verse does not require cutting all hands of each. And that hand is the right hand, by the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'faqṭa'ū aymānahumā' (cut off their right hands), which is a proof for us, and upon it consensus has been established, and the inherited sunnah has been practiced. So if it is cut once by theft, the place for cutting is lost, so what is cut on the third? Like if the right hand is cut by another cause, then he steals first? The cutting drops. Unlike flogging, for it is not lost thereby? The left? With a thousand times with?

Commentary
This is a major usul dispute concerning whether a command (صيغة الأمر) that is attached to a condition or description implies repetition (تكرار) every time the condition or description occurs. The passage presents two views: some say the command is put for repetition absolutely; others say it is not for repetition. If the attached element is a cause (علة), then by reason the command repeats with the cause (i.e., the effect repeats), but some deny that. The Hanafi position: the command form itself does not imply repetition; the repetition is by rational necessity (العقل) when the cause is present and obstacles are removed. The text then addresses objections: why does divorce (طلاق) not repeat with repeated entry? Because entry is not considered a cause for divorce in this context (the attachment is not for causality). Then why does theft not lead to cutting the hand a second time? Because cutting one hand removes the locus (المحل), so no further cutting is possible. Flogging, on the other hand, is repeatable because the locus remains. The author insists the dispute is verbal and that the Hanafi claim that repetition is by reason, not wording, is correct.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (ṣīghat al-amr): The imperative form (e.g., ifta'l, 'do')
التكرار (al-takrār): Repetition: performing the commanded act multiple times
العلة (al-ʿilla): The cause: the condition or description that triggers the command

C) Because repetition would lead to infinite divorces.
D) Because the wife's consent is needed for each divorce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the imperative form does not imply repetition; only rational causality can necessitate repetition when the condition is a cause and the locus remains.

Command Attached to Condition or Description: Does It Imply Repetition?
Dispute on whether the imperative implies repetition when attached to a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the imperative does not linguistically indicate repetition when attached to a condition, and distinguish between rational repetition (due to cause) and linguistic repetition.

Core Idea
The imperative is for a single request; repetition is rational only when the condition is a cause.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh dispute: does the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr) imply repetition when it is attached to a condition or description? The Hanafi school maintains that the imperative is placed only for requesting the action, not for repetition. However, some scholars argue that commands like 'When you rise for prayer, wash your faces' show repetition. We will explore the proofs on both sides and understand the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Contrast the two positions
Identify the two schools on whether the imperative attached to a condition indicates repetition absolutely (repetitionists) or not (Hanafis). Note that Hanafis allow rational repetition only if the condition is a cause.
The key distinction is between linguistic placement (al-waḍʿ) and rational necessity (al-ʿaql).
2
Examine the Hanafi proof from linguistic placement
The imperative form is only for requesting the action (ṭalab al-fiʿl), not for repetition. Example: 'If you enter the market, buy bread' does not imply a new purchase each entry.
This is based on the principle that the imperative is haqīqah for a single request.
3
Analyze the opponent's inductive argument and the Hanafi rebuttal
Opponents cite Quranic verses like wudu (5:6) and flogging (24:2) that seem to repeat with conditions. Hanafis respond that repetition in those is due to causality or external evidence, not the imperative itself.
The Hadith example of cutting the foot in a second theft shows that repetition is from sunna, not the verse.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In a case like 'If you enter the market, buy bread,' the repetitionist view requires a purchase each time; the Hanafi view requires only one unless the condition is a cause.
This illustrates how the dispute affects real rulings.

Issue: The imperative form attached to a condition or description is said to be placed for repetition with the repetition of the condition and description, absolutely, whether the condition or description is a cause or not. And it is said: The imperative attached is not for repetition, absolutely. If it is a cause, then does the command repeat with its repetition? There is disagreement rationally. The truth is yes, it repeats, and it is said no, it does not repeat. When disagreement is established in this manner, the claim of consensus regarding the cause, as in al-Mukhtasar and others, that repetition occurs with its repetition is wrong, and it is not correct to accuse the claimant of consensus of error, because the Hanafis say it does not repeat with the repetition of the condition even if it is a cause, since their intent is that it does not indicate by placement, only rational indication. Yes, after the disagreement is confirmed as the author narrated, consensus is negated, but it is extremely far-fetched to deny the ruling after the causality of the cause is established, except for those who reject analogy absolutely. Our proofs: first, it has preceded that the form is only for request, and the substance for reality, as such there is no repetition as preceded. Second, 'If you enter the market then buy such-and-such' does not imply repetition; otherwise it would be like 'whenever', but it is agreed that it is not like 'whenever'. As for repetition by the cause on which it is attached, it is due to the necessity of the repetition of the effect with its repetition, because non-concomitance is impossible. For the intended meaning is that it only repeats when obstacles are removed, and then non-concomitance is impossibly certain. Then this restriction is only for those who permit specification of the cause by an impediment, and this repetition is not by the wording but by reason. In al-Hashiya: not by consensus as Ibn al-Hajib claimed, and that is because consensus is not established; rather disagreement is established. If it is said that the one who said it is by the wording meant that attachment to the description and condition suggests causality linguistically, and the effect repeats with the repetition of the cause, but since this indication is probable, it might fail to follow the indicator. I say: then the dispute becomes verbal, for the intent of the majority who deny repetition with regard to the wording is that it is not placed for it. So understand. If you ask: why then is divorce not repeated with the repetition of entry when attached to it due to causality? He said: divorce is not repeated with the entry attached to it because its causality for divorce is not considered. If you ask: if the attached thing is a cause, repetition must occur with its repetition, then why do the Hanafis not cut the hand of the thief in the third theft, even though theft is the cause for cutting, and they flog in zina every time after flogging, even though both are causes? We say: theft is a cause for cutting one hand, for two hands are not cut for one theft by consensus. It is supported that confronting plural with plural requires the division of individuals among individuals, so the meaning is: cut the hand of the male thief and the hand of the female thief; the verse does not require cutting all the hands of each. And that hand is the right hand, according to the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'cut off their right hands', which is a proof for us, and upon it consensus is also concluded, and the inherited sunna proceeds accordingly. So when it is cut once due to theft, the place for cutting is lost, so what is cut in the third? Just as if the right hand is cut for another reason then he steals first, cutting is waived. Unlike flogging, for it does not eliminate the place, which is the body, so he is flogged a second time if he commits zina. If you ask: why is the foot cut in the second theft? He said: cutting the foot in the second is initially by the sunna. In al-Hashiya: al-Shafi'i and al-Tabarani narrated from the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions): 'If a thief steals, cut off his hand, then if he steals, cut off his foot.' Thus in al-Taqrir. Or the repetitionists regarding the attached command said: it is established by induction in the commands of the Law that the attached repeats with the repetition of that to which it is attached, such as His statement: 'When you rise for prayer, wash your faces...' and His statement: 'The female and male fornicator, flog each one of them one hundred lashes' and His statement: 'If you are in a state of janabah, then purify yourselves.' We said: repetition in the cause is conceded and does not benefit you, and repetition in other than it occurs by a specific evidence other than the command indicating repetition; this also does not benefit you. That is why Hajj does not repeat even though attached to capability, because capability is not a cause. God said: 'And due to Allah from the people is pilgrimage to the House, for whoever finds a way to it.' In this example reflect, reflect, O denier of repetition in the cause. They said: if repetition occurs in the cause, then the condition is more deserving of repetition, for it does not multiply in itself, so if it multiplies likewise, the conditioned must multiply. So why does the conditioned not repeat with the repetition of the condition? Because repetition is a multiplicity. We said: repetition occurs with regard to existence, not essence, and the cause necessitates it: whenever it exists, it exists, unlike the condition, for it does not necessitate it, so it does not repeat with its repetition. Multiplicity by virtue of the multiplicity of the condition is only with regard to essence: if the essence of the condition multiplies, the conditioned multiplies necessarily. So reflect.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh dispute about whether the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr) indicates repetition (takrār) when it is attached (mu'allaq) to a condition (sharṭ) or description (ṣifa). The author presents two main positions: one group says the imperative is placed for repetition whenever the condition/description repeats, absolutely (muṭlaqan) whether the condition is a cause (ʿilla) or not. Another group says it is not placed for repetition; rather, if the attachment is a cause, then rational necessity demands repetition of the effect, but this is not a linguistic indication. The Hanafis are among the latter group. The author rebuts the claim of consensus (ijmāʿ) on this issue, showing that disagreement exists. He then provides Hanafi proofs: (1) The imperative form is only for requesting the action, not for repetition. (2) Examples like 'If you enter the market, buy such-and-such' do not imply repetition, unlike 'kullamā' (whenever). Repetition from a cause is rational, not by wording. The discussion extends to why divorce does not repeat with repeated entry (because the marriage contract ends after first divorce), and why hadd punishments differ: theft causes only one hand cut, and the place is lost; flogging repeats because the body remains. The cutting of the foot in second theft is by sunna, not by the verse. Finally, the repetitionists argue by induction from Quranic verses like wudu and flogging, but the Hanafis respond that repetition in those is due to causality or specific evidence, not the imperative itself. Hajj does not repeat even though attached to capability because capability is not a cause. The passage ends by distinguishing between repetition of existence (which follows cause) and multiplicity of essence (which follows condition when the condition itself multiplies).

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (ṣīghat al-amr): The imperative form in Arabic, which is placed only for requesting the action (ṭalab al-fiʿl), not for repetition.
تكرار (takrār): Repetition of the commanded action upon repetition of the condition or cause; the text distinguishes between linguistic repetition (by wording) and rational repetition (by cause).
علة (ʿilla): The cause or effective reason that necessitates the legal ruling; when the command is attached to a cause, the effect repeats with the cause by rational necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, does the imperative 'If you enter the market, buy bread' require a separate purchase each time the market is entered?
A) Yes, because the command is repeated with the condition.
B) No, because the condition is not a cause, so the command is not repeated.
C) Only if the speaker intends repetition.
D) Yes, because of induction from Quranic verses.

Key Takeaway
The imperative does not linguistically imply repetition; repetition occurs only by rational necessity when the condition is a cause.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
15 min
The Imperative Form: Immediacy or Deferment?
Usul dispute on the indication of the imperative form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether the imperative form indicates immediacy or deferment, apply the Hanafi rebuttals to the proponents' arguments, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf in the ruling on delaying Hajj.

Core Idea
The imperative form indicates only future demand; context determines immediacy or delay.

This passage dives into a classic usul dispute: does the mere imperative (ṣīghat al-amr) require immediate action or permit delay? The Hanafi school takes a nuanced stance, arguing that the linguistic form itself signals neither. Instead, it simply demands the act in the future, leaving timing to external evidence. This lesson unpacks the five arguments from proponents of immediacy and the Hanafi counterarguments, culminating in a practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) regarding delaying the Hajj obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Core Hanafi Position
Identify what the Hanafis claim about the imperative form: it indicates neither fawr nor tarakhi, but only a future demand. Note the distinction between hay'ah (form) and maddah (material).
The form is for mere demand; the material is for the reality of the act.
2
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Locate the practical dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on delaying Hajj. Understand how the usul difference affects whether delay is sinful and whether testimony of delay is accepted.
The thamarah shows the real-world impact of the usul disagreement.
3
Work Through the Five Opposing Arguments
List the five arguments from proponents of fawr: (1) example of 'give me a drink', (2) induction from reports and constructions, (3) prohibition implies immediacy, (4) Qur'anic verse blaming Iblis, (5) delaying leads to impossible obligation.
Each argument is countered by the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the Hanafi Rebuttals
For each argument, articulate the Hanafi response: (1) context-dependent, (2) not linguistic convention but inference, (3) prohibition is subordinate, (4) verse is restricted, (5) solution is delegation (tafwid).
Focus on how the rebuttals preserve the principle that the form alone gives no timing.
5
Conclude the Preferred Position
Summarize why the Hanafi view is considered stronger: consensus on zakat, logical consistency, and the concept of delegation avoiding impossibility.
The tarjih rests on the strength of rebuttals and practical consensus.

The one who comes in the later year is a judge according to him, but it is not so; rather the disagreement is new. The proof of Imam Muhammad is clear, and the proof of Abu Yusuf – may Allah have mercy on him – is that life until the second year is uncertain, so delay is exposure to abandonment, thus it is unlawful. But if he reaches a second year and performs Hajj, his sin is lifted as stated by Sadr al-Shari'ah, because the sin is essentially in leaving the obligation, and it is attributed to delay only because it is a means to it; when the means no longer exists, he is not sinful. So the fruit of the disagreement appears in accepting testimony of delay until the second year: is it accepted before performance in the second year or not? So ponder. And al-Sakkaki and al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani chose the obligation of immediate action or resolve as in the expanded obligatory duty. The Imam was undecided whether it is for immediacy or the common denominator between immediacy and postponement; if he performs immediately, he is absolved with certainty; if he delays, sin is possible, so immediacy is obligatory as a precaution, and postponement is not possibly obligatory. And it is said to be suspended absolutely in immediacy and postponement, the possibility of its obligation meaning postponement, perhaps he sins if he hastens. For us is like what preceded in repetition: that the form is merely for demand, and the material for the reality as such, so it indicates only demand in the future in any part of it. And for us also, if it were for immediacy, the obligation would be timed at the first moment after the command, and otherwise it would be considered qada', and paying zakat in the second year would be qada', which is contrary to consensus. The proponents of immediacy said: First: 'Give me a drink' is for immediacy, and all commands are of one pattern in indication because they are for the form. We respond: we do not accept that it is for immediacy by linguistic convention, but rather it is understood by context, which is the request for drink at the time of need and onset of thirst. And they said: Second: Every informant and every initiator intends the present by induction, so likewise the command indicates it, annexing it to the most common in reports and constructions. The first response: I say: of the absolute universal report, its judgment applies in reality whether past, present, or future, and it is a reality according to Ibn Sina and his followers, so we do not accept that every informant and initiator intends the present. This chain is not in its place, for Ibn Sina is not among the men of this discourse, and he did not claim linguistic convention and reality; rather he only intended to obtain the meaning of a proposition of this nature, and he explicitly stated that the reality of the absolute in custom is persistence in the time of the attribute. And the past report does not require concurrence with the present, nor does the reporter intend it, but rather pastness absolutely, whether concurrent or distant; likewise the command in the future, i.e., it may be so, and this chain is good. Second response: As it is said, the present in the command is the time of demand, for the demand is in the present, and that does not require that the time of the demanded be present. The discussion was about that, and the evidence, if complete, benefits the first. I say: the adherent means the time of the object of the report and construction is present, and the reporter and initiator intend the occurrence of their objects in it; so likewise the object of the command, and this is very apparent. Third response: that it is a linguistic analogy, and it is prohibited. If it is said: the sum of the evidence is annexing the command to other constructions and reports, and annexation is not analogy but induction, like inducting the raising of the subject, for it is annexing the less to the more frequent. I say: in induction of a genus, it is necessary to trace the specific individuals present before the inductor; here, it is necessary to trace the types of construction and report; with the existence of the command and not tracing it, there is no induction, but rather there is induction of some types and analogy of the command upon them; so nothing remains except analogy. Fourth response: that the present in the command is impossible, for what is obtained cannot be demanded, and the necessary from the evidence is the present, so it is not possible except the future, either immediately as you hold, or after as it is said, or absolutely as you say. So the necessary from the evidence contradicts your claim, and the claimed is not necessary. So this response is a prevention of the complete approximation or a general refutation by saying that if it were complete, impossibility would follow. It is said: he did not mean the present moment so that what you mentioned would follow; rather he meant parts of the late past and early future, which is the customary present; immediacy is included in it, so no impossibility follows, and the approximation is complete. I say: if what this speaker said were correct, the command in the object would be linked to the customary present, and it would be like present tense forms, which is contrary to the consensus of Arabic scholars. Immediacy is only the early part of the future customarily, so the problematic consequence follows backwards; so ponder. Yes: if it were said in the evidence that the origin requires the command to be for the present because it is more frequent, and since concurrence is not possible, and nearness is like the present, the command is made for it by way of working within the bounds of possibility, it would not be far-fetched, as was said regarding the circumstantial accusative governed by the verb: its origin is to be concurrent with the agent, and when not in the past, it is made to be near. So understand. And they said: Third: The prohibition is for immediacy, and the command is a prohibition of its opposite, so it is also for immediacy; otherwise, the elevation of two contradictories would follow. And similar has preceded in the issue of repetition with the answer that this prohibition is subordinate to the command and not for immediacy. And they said: Fourth: His statement – exalted be He – addressing Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating when I commanded you?' [al-A'raf: 12] is blame for not hastening to prostrate, so it is for immediacy, and commands are all of one pattern, so the form is for it. We respond: The command to prostrate is not absolute, but rather restricted by His statement: 'So when I have proportioned him and breathed into him of My spirit, fall down to him in prostration' [al-Hijr: 29]. The discussion was about the absolute command; the restricted command depends on the requirement of the restriction regarding immediacy or postponement; here it is restricted to immediacy, so it is for it; so ponder. And they said: Fifth: If it were not for immediacy and delay were permitted, then either to a specific time – a proof for it – and if it is said the specific time is old age, he said: Old age, for example, does not specify, for how many a young man dies suddenly, so old age is not realized and the obligation is missed; and how many an old man lives a long time, so he can perform the obligation after old age, so old age is not suitable as a specification for delay; or to the last times of possibility, which is unknown, so delaying to it would entail obligation of the impossible. We respond: This is refuted by the permissibility of explicitly stating delay, for it is permissible by consensus, while the premises of the evidence also apply to it. The solution to the evidence is that the impossible only follows by obligating delay to the last times of possibility, not by delegating to him to come at any time from the times of ability; so ponder. So it is not valid. Now, what is in Allah's knowledge is unknown to him, and this is close to this reasoning of the proponents of immediacy. And Shaykh al-Haddad refuted it by saying that the mufti can answer: it is permissible for you to delay with the possibility of sin due to your death before reaching the other year, and then you are ruled as sinful. The sum is that it is permissible for him to delay with the absence...
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute on whether the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr) indicates immediacy (fawr) or deferment (tarākhī). The Hanafi position, represented by Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, is that the command does not by itself indicate either; it simply demands action in the future. The entry point is the practical ruling on delaying Hajj: Abu Yusuf holds that delay is prohibited because life is uncertain, but if one performs Hajj in the second year, the sin is lifted. Imam Muhammad allows delay. The usul debate then expands: proponents of fawr (likely Shafi'is and others) present five arguments. First, the example of 'give me a drink' implies immediacy, but Hanafis respond that this is due to context (qarīna), not the form itself. Second, they argue that all reports and constructions intend the present, but Hanafis counter that this is not linguistically necessary, and Ibn Sina's definition of absolute propositions applies to all times. Third, they argue that a prohibition is for immediacy, and command is prohibition of its opposite; Hanafis reply that the prohibition follows the command and does not indicate immediacy. Fourth, they cite the Qur'anic verse blaming Iblis for not prostrating immediately, but Hanafis note that verse is restricted by another verse specifying 'when I have proportioned him'. Fifth, they argue that allowing delay leads to impossible consequences (obligation until an unknown time), but Hanafis respond that the solution is delegation (tafwīḍ) to act at any time of ability, not postponement to an unknown limit. The passage concludes with Shaykh al-Haddad's response that allows delay with the risk of sin. Throughout, the Hanafi position is that the command's form only indicates a future demand, and any implication of immediacy comes from external evidence.

Key Terms
الفور (al-fawr): Immediacy; the requirement that an obligatory act be performed at the earliest possible time after the command.
التراخي (al-tarākhī): Deferment; the allowance to postpone an obligatory act to a later time.
الهيئة (al-hay'ah): The form or structure of the imperative verb, which indicates mere demand according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis in this passage, what does the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr) indicate by itself?
A) Immediacy (fawr)
B) Deferment (tarākhī)
C) Only a demand for action in the future
D) Both immediacy and deferment equally

Key Takeaway
The imperative form alone does not require immediate action; timing is derived from external evidence, and the Hanafi position allows flexibility unless restricted.

Does Prohibition Linguistically Imply Invalidity?
Examining whether nahy linguistically implies fasād

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that nahy does not linguistically indicate fasād and evaluate the opposing argument.

Core Idea
Prohibition is a request to abandon – it says nothing about the validity of the act.

In Hanafi usul, the linguistic meaning of commands and prohibitions is foundational. Does the word 'nahy' itself imply that the prohibited act is invalid? This passage examines that question, settling on the Hanafi position that nahy only means 'do not do it,' without any inherent consequence on the act's legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
What exactly is being debated? Is the linguistic meaning of nahy (prohibition) such that it necessarily entails the invalidity (fasād) of the prohibited act?
Focus on the pure linguistic claim, not the legal ruling.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi position is that nahy does not linguistically indicate fasād. The proof given is a hypothetical: one can say 'If you do this, its rulings occur, but do not do it; if you do, I will punish you' without contradiction. This shows nahy only communicates a request to abandon.
The example demonstrates that nahy is consistent with the act still producing effects.
3
Present the opposing argument
Some scholars argue that because command (amr) indicates validity (sihha), and prohibition is its opposite, it must indicate the opposite of validity, i.e., invalidity. This is a logical opposition argument.
This argument relies on the principle that opposites have opposite implications.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: opposites do not necessarily have opposite implications. Command may indicate validity due to additional factors (e.g., rational or legal), but prohibition only indicates a request to abandon; it does not imply anything about effects. The claim that amr indicates validity is itself contested – it may be a legal, not linguistic, implication.
The rebuttal uses linguistic examples and logical reasoning to sever the assumed link.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in linguistic analysis, the word 'nahy' does not by itself imply that the prohibited act is invalid. To establish fasād, one needs additional evidence. This affects usul reasoning when deriving rulings from prohibitive texts.
The Hanafi school thus distinguishes between the linguistic meaning of nahy and the legal ruling of invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
توقف الإمام فيه وهذا إنما يتيسر لو أبدى عدم صحة نقل الإجماع.
مسألة
النهي هل يدل على الفساد وهو عدم ترتب الحكم عليه (لغة) أم لا يدل (المختار لا) يدل لغة عليه، وقيل : يدل لنا أن فساد الشيء عبارة عن سلب أحكامه، وليس المدلول (في لفظ النهي سوى طلب الترك) حتماً (وهو لا يستلزم ذلك أي عدم ترتب الأحكام (قطعاً) وكيف لا ومن البين أنه لو قال : إذا فعلت هذا الشيء ترتبت أحكامه، لكن لا تفعل، إن فعلت عاقبتك، لم يعد مناقضاً لغة وعرفاً، ولم يحكم بتغير النهي عن موضعه، وإنكار هذا مكابرة، القائلون بالفساد لغة (قالوا: الأمر يقتضي (الصحة) وهو ظاهر جداً فإنه لا يأمر عاقل بأمر لا يكون هو صحيحاً ومعتبراً عنده والنهي نقيضه، ومقتضى النقيض نقيض المقتضى) فمقتضى النهي نقيض مقتضى الأمر وهو الفساد (والجواب : أن المتقابلين لا يجب تقابل أحكامهما فلا نسلم أن مقتضى النقيض نقيض المقتضى ؛ فيجوز أن يكون مقتضى الأمر الصحة غير اقتضاء مع النهي الفساد، بل مع اقتضاء الصحة كيف ومقتضى الأمر إيقاع الفعل، ولا يكون واقعاً إلا مترتب الآثار، بخلاف النهي، فإنه يقتضي الكفَّ عنه وهو لا يقتضي عدم ترتب الآثار فافهم على أن للاقتضاء مع عدم الاقتضاء نوعاً من (التقابل فحينئذ يجوز أن يكون الأمر مقتضياً للصحة والنهي لا يكون مقتضياً لها وفيه أيضاً تناف في الأحكام، وفيه أن المستدل ادعى وجوب التنافي بين مقتضييهما ومقتضى الأمر الصحة فيكون مقتضى النهي منافيها وهو الفساد، فإن منع وجوب التنافي بينهما فهو الوجه الأول، وإلا فليس في اليد منع فتدبر. وقد يجاب بأن هذا قياس في اللغة ورد بأنه استدلال باستقراء حال المتنافيين فتدبر، وقد يجاب أيضاً بمنع اقتضاء الأمر لغة الصحة فإنه لطلب المأخذ ولا يوجب ترتب الأحكام وإنما يجب شرعاً أو عقلاً وفيه أنه لطلب الإيقاع للمأخذ في العين، وظاهر أن الوجودية هي ترتب الثمرات والآثار فهو مقتض لترتب الثمرات في نظر الآمر ، وإلا لما صح منه طلب الإيقاع فتأمل فيه .
The third article on linguistic principles. The Imam [Abu Hanifa?] hesitated in this, and this is only possible if he showed the invalidity of transmitting consensus. Issue: Does prohibition indicate invalidity, which is the non-occurrence of the ruling upon it, linguistically or not? The chosen [position] is that it does not indicate it linguistically, and it is said: it does indicate. For us, the invalidity of a thing is an expression of the negation of its rulings, and the denotation in the word of prohibition is nothing other than a request for abandonment decisively, and it does not necessitate that, i.e., non-occurrence of rulings, at all. How not? And it is clear that if he said: 'If you do this thing, its rulings occur, but do not do it; if you do, I will punish you,' it would not be considered contradictory linguistically or customarily, and it would not be judged that the prohibition has changed from its place. Denying this is obstinacy. Those who say invalidity linguistically said: Command requires validity, and this is very apparent, for no intelligent person commands something that is not valid and considered in his view, and prohibition is its opposite, and the requirement of the opposite is the opposite of the requirement. So the requirement of prohibition is the opposite of the requirement of command, which is invalidity. The answer: Opposites do not necessitate the opposition of their rulings, so we do not accept that the requirement of the opposite is the opposite of the requirement. So it is possible that the requirement of command is validity without requiring with the prohibition invalidity; rather, with requiring validity, how? And the requirement of command is the occurrence of the act, and it does not occur except with the occurrence of effects, unlike prohibition, for it requires abstinence from it, and it does not require non-occurrence of effects. Understand that for requirement with non-requirement there is a type of opposition. Then it is possible that command requires validity and prohibition does not require it, and in that there is also contradiction in rulings. And in that, the arguer claimed the necessity of contradiction between their requirements, and the requirement of command is validity, so the requirement of prohibition would be contradictory to it, which is invalidity. If he denies the necessity of contradiction between them, that is the first aspect; otherwise, there is no prevention in hand, so ponder. And it may be answered that this is analogy in language, and it is rebutted by saying it is reasoning by induction of the state of the two opposites, so ponder. And it may also be answered by denying that command linguistically requires validity, for it is a request for the object [of the action] and does not necessitate the occurrence of rulings; rather, it is obligatory by law or reason. And in it, that it is a request for the occurrence of the object in the essence, and it is apparent that existentiality is the occurrence of fruits and effects, so it is a requirer for the occurrence of fruits in the view of the commander; otherwise, his request for occurrence would not be valid, so reflect on it.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic usul dispute: whether the prohibition (nahy) linguistically indicates invalidity (fasād). The author presents the mukhtār (preferred) position that nahy does not indicate fasād linguistically, because its meaning is simply a request to abandon, which does not entail that the act's effects are void. He gives the example: a person says 'If you do this, its rulings will apply, but don't do it; if you do, I'll punish you' — this is not contradictory. Then he states the opposing view: command indicates validity, and since nahy is its opposite, it should indicate the opposite of validity, i.e., invalidity. The author refutes this by noting that opposites do not necessarily entail opposite rulings; command may imply validity while nahy simply does not imply anything about effects. He further points out that the argument of those who claim nahy indicates invalidity is based on an analogy (qiyās) in language, which is rejected because it relies on induction of opposites. Another answer is that command does not linguistically require validity; it is merely a request, and the binding nature of rulings is legal or rational, not linguistic. The passage thus upholds the Hanafi position that nahy does not linguistically imply fasād.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): Non-occurrence of rulings on an act (عدم ترتب الحكم عليه).
النهي (al-nahy): A request for abandonment (talab al-tark) made in a decisive manner.
المختار (al-mukhtār): The preferred or chosen position among scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen (mukhtār) position in this passage, does the word 'nahy' (prohibition) linguistically indicate that the prohibited act is invalid (fasād)?
A) Yes, it necessarily indicates invalidity.
B) No, it only indicates a request to abandon, without implying any effect on validity.
C) It indicates validity instead of invalidity.
D) It indicates invalidity only in legal contexts, not linguistically.

Key Takeaway
Prohibition (nahy) linguistically means only a request to abandon; it does not inherently indicate the invalidity (fasād) of the prohibited act.

Does Prohibition Indicate Invalidity? The Hanafi View
Examining whether nahy indicates fasād in Islamic law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether nahy indicates fasād, identify the opposing views, and evaluate the key arguments and counterarguments presented in the text.

Core Idea
Nahy does not inherently indicate fasād; it indicates validity, and any invalidity arises from an external description.

This passage dives into a fundamental usul al-fiqh debate: does a legal prohibition (nahy) automatically render an act invalid (fasād)? The Hanafi school famously holds that it does not – the act remains valid in itself, though sinful. This lesson unpacks the dispute between the Hanafis and scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Razi, using two key arguments: the consensus of the scholars and the wisdom of the Prohibitor. By the end, you will understand the subtle distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic invalidity and its practical effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue and the positions
Read the passage and note the core question: does nahy indicate fasād? Identify the three positions: (1) nahy indicates fasād absolutely, (2) only in worship, (3) does not indicate fasād at all (Hanafi position).
The 'chosen' (mukhtār) position in the text is the first, but the author will later side with the Hanafi view.
2
Analyze the Hanafi claim that nahy indicates validity
The Hanafis argue that nahy necessitates validity (ṣiḥḥa) in all legal matters. Find the evidence they present, such as the example of divorce during menstruation and the principle that acts are valid unless a specific impediment is established.
The key distinction is between intrinsic fasād (bi al-dhāt) and extrinsic fasād (bi al-ghayr).
3
Examine the opponents' argument (Ghazali/Razi)
Ghazali and Razi argue that in worship, prohibition must indicate invalidity because worship is commanded; a prohibition would contradict the command. Identify how they use the supposed contradiction to support their position.
They limit this to worship, not transactions.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that prohibition can attach to a description (e.g., a specific condition) without affecting the intrinsic validity of the commanded act. Thus, there is no contradiction. For example, divorce during menstruation is prohibited but valid.
This response resolves the apparent conflict by distinguishing between the act's essence and its accompanying attributes.
5
Consider the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Contrast the outcomes: In Hanafi fiqh, a prohibited transaction (e.g., sale during Friday prayer call) is valid but sinful; in the opposing view, it would be invalid. Understand how this affects fatwa and daily practice.
This thamarah is crucial for understanding the impact of the usul dispute on furu' al-fiqh.

وله أفراد كاملة وناقصة ينصرف إلى الكامل، وأما ههنا فالنهي ليس موضوعاً للقبح ولا للفساد وإنما يلزم لضرورة حكمة الناهي، فهو من باب الاقتضاء أو الدلالة الالتزامية التابعة ولا انصراف فيهما إلى انفراد على الوصف فلا تضاد لتغاير المحلين للمأمورية والنهيية وهذا الجواب إنما ينتهض من منكر استدعائه الفساد مطلقاً، وأما من قبل مدعي الفساد أن هذا إنما ينتج أن الفساد
Issue: Does prohibition indicate invalidity legally or not? The chosen [position] is yes, it indicates absolutely in worship and transactions. And it is said: it indicates only in worship, not in transactions. Imam Ghazali and Imam Razi inclined to that. And the holder of this school [the Hanafi?] says that it does not indicate invalidity in transactions, and according to some it indicates validity in them, and Sheikh Ibn al-Humam chose that. Then there is discussion here, because if he means intrinsic invalidity, which is non-occurrence of fruits which is nullity in our terminology, then the Hanafis have held that it does not necessitate invalidity, rather it necessitates validity in legal matters, whether worship or transactions, and the author will choose that. So his saying 'the chosen' is not correct here. And if he means more general, whether intrinsic invalidity or extrinsic, which is that committing it necessitates sin due to essence or accompanying a description or adjacency, then it is the same, and it is valid to make the second school opposite to it, for no one has gone to deny this invalidity even in transactions. If you say the intended is intrinsic invalidity and the discussion is when the prohibition is on the essence not due to a description as clarified in al-Mukhtasar, I say: a useful marginal note is transmitted here for the prohibition being absolute, whether for its essence or due to description. Also, the attachment of prohibition to the essence itself in legal matters is not valid according to us, so worship does not enter into the subject of the issue, so it is not valid to transmit the school as opposite to the first here, and there is no answer to this discussion except to say: the intended is that prohibition as prohibition without observing a diverting indicator indicates intrinsic invalidity, and in the second school there is indication only in worship. As for legal matters, although they do not indicate according to us, the legal nature diverts it. So ponder, for it is a subject worthy of contemplation for us. First, the scholars of the lands have continuously inferred invalidity from prohibition absolutely, whether worship or transaction, so its indication of invalidity is a matter of consensus. And in it, as will come, this is accepted in non-legal matters; as for legal matters, it is not accepted. And if the claimant makes it that it, as it is, disregarding external indicators, indicates invalidity, the conclusion is that the scholars of the lands used to infer from the very prohibition intrinsic invalidity, and they needed for extrinsic invalidity a diverting indicator. So it is for invalidity legally. But the Hanafis' inference that it is for intrinsic validity and invalidity due to description, if proven, indicates that the requirement of the very prohibition in legal matters is that legal nature is invalidity, so ponder. And for us, secondly, the wisdom of the prohibitor necessitates the ugliness of the prohibited thing, for the wise only prohibits obscenity, so the ugliness is intrinsic, and the ugly in the view of the wise does not have fruits occur at all, so invalidity is necessary. But the wisdom of the prohibitor only necessitates absolute ugliness, not intrinsic ugliness such that intrinsic invalidity follows. And if he seeks help by saying the absolute turns to the perfect individual, and perfect ugliness is that which is intrinsic, then that is when the indicator is used in a matching way and it has perfect and imperfect individuals, it turns to the perfect. But here, prohibition is not established for ugliness or invalidity; rather, it is necessary by necessity of the wisdom of the prohibitor, so it is from the category of requirement or implied dependent indication, and there is no turning in them to the perfect alone. Rather, it will become clear to you, God willing, that the wisdom of the prohibitor necessitating that the prohibited thing be possible indicates that prohibition in legal matters indicates intrinsic goodness and ugliness for other than itself. So wait. Those who say there is no indication of invalidity absolutely said: If prohibition indicated invalidity, then explicit statement of validity would be contradictory to its concept, and that is invalid, for we know with certainty that if the Lawgiver said 'Do not divorce during menstruation, and if you divorce it occurs and its rulings apply, and if you do I will punish you,' it would be valid. The answer is denial of the implication, for explicit statement of validity changes it. And you may formulate their proof: if it indicated invalidity, then explicit statement of validity would be contradictory to its concept in reason, just as 'riding' is contradictory to the concept of 'lion' in reason, but it is not so due to certain knowledge of what was mentioned. However, there is reflection on the invalidity of the consequent. Those who restrict to invalidity in worship said: worship is commanded, so it cannot be prohibited due to contradiction between them. The answer: it is possible that the prohibition refers to the description, so they are commanded intrinsically, and the prohibition is due to their inclusion of the description, so there is no contradiction due to the distinct loci of the commanded aspect and the prohibited aspect. And this answer only arises from one who denies its requiring invalidity absolutely. As for from the one who claims invalidity, this only produces that invalidity...
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of whether nahy indicates fasād, now in the legal (sharʿī) context. The author presents several positions: the chosen (mukhtār) is that nahy indicates invalidity absolutely in both worship and transactions. Another position restricts it to worship only, inclined to by Ghazali and Razi. The Hanafī position, according to the text and as held by Ibn al-Humam, is that nahy does not necessitate invalidity but rather validity in all legal matters. The author then discusses the ambiguity of what 'fasād' means: intrinsic (bi al-dhāt) or extrinsic (bi al-ghayr). The Hanafīs maintain that in legal matters, nahy indicates intrinsic validity, and any invalidity arises from a description or external factor. The passage also provides arguments: first, that scholars have historically inferred invalidity from prohibition, claiming consensus; second, from the wisdom (ḥikma) of the prohibitor that the prohibited is ugly and thus invalid. The opponents counter with the example of divorce during menstruation: the Prophet forbade it but it still takes effect. The Hanafī response is that the explicit statement of validity overrides any implication of invalidity. Another argument from those who limit invalidity to worship is that worship is commanded, so it cannot be simultaneously prohibited. The author replies that the prohibition attaches to a description, not the essence, so there is no real contradiction. This passage is dense and reflects deep usulī debate.

Key Terms
الفساد بالذات (al-fasād bi al-dhāt): Intrinsic invalidity: an act's rulings do not occur due to its own essence.
الفساد بالغير (al-fasād bi al-ghayr): Extrinsic invalidity: an act is invalid due to a description or accompanying factor that necessitates sin.
الحكمة (al-ḥikma): The wisdom or rationale behind a ruling; here it is used as evidence that the prohibitor's wisdom necessitates ugliness and thus invalidity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the effect of a prohibition (nahy) on the validity of a legal act?
A) It necessitates invalidity of the act.
B) It necessitates validity of the act, and any invalidity is due to an external description.
C) It only indicates validity in worship but invalidity in transactions.
D) It indicates that the act is both sinful and invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle is that prohibition does not entail invalidity; an act can be sinful yet valid, and any invalidity must be traced to an external factor.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Does Prohibition Indicate Invalidity? Hanafi Usul Analysis
Usul dispute on nahy and fasad with rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on nahy indicating fasad by distinguishing between acts of worship and transactions, and by critiquing opposing arguments using the distinction between essence and description.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition does not necessarily invalidate; the act's essential validity depends on whether the prohibition targets the essence or a description.

This lesson explores a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: does a legal prohibition (nahy) automatically render the prohibited act invalid (fasid)? The Hanafi school takes a nuanced position, arguing that invalidity is not an inevitable consequence, especially in transactions (mu'amalat) where worldly benefits remain. The passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut dissects the arguments, rebuttals, and counter-rebuttals, culminating in the relied-upon Hanafi view. By understanding this debate, you will sharpen your ability to analyze how textual commands interact with the underlying wisdom (hikmah) of the Lawgiver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and opposing views
From the passage, extract the core Hanafi claim: nahy does not indicate fasad absolutely. Note the two opposing views: (1) nahy indicates fasad absolutely, and (2) nahy indicates fasad only in 'ibadat.
Pay attention to how the text frames the disagreement – it is about whether the prohibition attaches to the essence (dhat) or a description (wasf).
2
Trace the Hanafi evidence from the wisdom of the Prohibitor
The Hanafis argue that the wisdom (hikmah) of the Lawgiver only requires that the prohibited act be possible, not inherently ugly. An objection claims that wisdom demands essential ugliness, but the Hanafis respond that the implication is not literal and does not divert to the perfect instance.
Understand the logical chain: prohibition implies ugliness, but ugliness does not necessarily entail invalidity, especially if the act has essential benefit.
3
Analyze the response to the claim of consensus
The opposing side cites consensus (ijma) that scholars used nahy to deduce fasad. The Hanafis restrict this consensus to non-shar'iyyat (non-religious matters). In shar'iyyat, the prohibition is often on a description, not the essence.
This shows how Hanafis limit the scope of consensus based on their usul principles.
4
Examine the distinction between 'ibadat and mu'amalat using Ibn al-Humam's argument
Ibn al-Humam argues that 'ibadat lack worldly benefit (only reward), so prohibition destroys their purpose, making them invalid. Mu'amalat retain worldly benefits (e.g., ownership in sale), so they remain valid despite prohibition.
This is the key practical distinction: the nature of the act's benefit determines whether prohibition invalidates.
5
Evaluate the final Hanafi conclusion and counter-critique
The passage ends with the author (Ibn Abd al-Shakur?) defending the Hanafi position against the objection that prohibition itself negates validity. He argues that in shar'iyyat, prohibition necessitates validity, not invalidity, and the burden of proof lies on the claimant of fasad.
Reflect on how the author reframes the debate: the default is validity unless evidence shows the prohibition targets the essence.

لازم في العبادات المنهية، وأما أنه ليس في المعاملات فلا يدل عليه أصلاً، بل يجوز أن يكون هناك لدليل آخر فتدبر على أن المعاملات قد تكون واجبة أيضاً، فهي مأمور بها، فلا تكون منهياً عنها كالعبادات، فانتقض الدليل ويمكن دفعه بأن المعاملات بعض منها غير مأمور به، فيجوز أن يكون منهياً عنه بخلاف العبادات فإن كلها مأمور بها، فتنافي النهي، كذا في «الحاشية»، ويرد مثله على أصل الدليل فإن العبادات منها ما هي مندوبة فلا تكون مأموراً بها إلا أن يراد بالمأمور به أعم، ثم قال بهذا يندفع أن المعاملات مباحة، فلا تكون منهياً عنها للتضاد، ولا يظهر وجه الدفع إلا أن منها ما هي حرام فيتعلق به النهي، وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإن دعوى المستدل أن النهي في المعاملات لا يقتضي الفساد بخلاف العبادات للتضاد في المأمورية والنهي والمعاملات غير مأمورة فتوجه الكلام فثبت أنها غير مأمورة لكنها مباحة البتة، فينافيه النهي، فإن أريد أن منها ما هو غير مباح بالذات فقد لزم الفساد فيها أيضاً، وهو خلاف مذهب المستدل وتم ،النقض وإن أريد أنها حرام لأجل الوصف فتكون مباحة لذاتها، فالنهي مضاد لها، وإن فرق بأن الإباحة لذاتها والنهي للوصف انقلب على أصل الدليل، وجوب النقض بد أن يكون بحيث لا ينقلب أصلاً فتدبر فحينئذ قد بان لك أن الجواب المعتمد هو إحداث التغاير في المحل، وشيد أركان الاستدلال الشيخ ابن الهمام أن المقصود في العبادات الثواب، فإذا نهى عنها صار ارتكابها موجباً للعقاب، فخلا العبادة عن ثمرتها بالنهي، فلا تكون مشروعة أصلاً، وأما المعاملات فلا بعد أن يقول الناهي: جعلت هذا الشيء سبباً لهذا، لكن لا تفعله، ولو فعلت عاقبتك، لا تخلو المشروعية الذاتية عن فائدتها في الدنيا، وإن كان موجباً للعقاب في الآخرة، كالبيع، فإن حكمه الملك ويثبت مع الحرمة والعبادات ليس لها ثمرة دنيوية، بل ثمرتها تكون الثواب لا غير وقد انعدم بالنهي فلا يصح، وقال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية» في شرح المنار ما ذكره في العبادات صحيح، وينبغي أن تكون المعاملات أيضاً كذلك فإن النافي للصحة متحقق وهو النهي، وما ذكره من المثال ففيه دليل صارف عن مقتضى النهي . ويقول هذا العبد ما ذكره الشيخ ابن الهمام مندفع فإنه هب أن المقصود في العبادات الثواب، لكن لا نسلم أنه ينافي تعلق النهي الذي موجبه العقاب، فإنه يجوز أن يثاب ويعاقب على فعل واحد، فإنه لما جوزنا أن يكون الشيء عبادة ومشروعاً في نفسه ويكون منهياً وغير مشروع بوصفه، فإذا أتى المكلف بهذا الفعل استحق لأن يعطى أجر نفس الفعل، ويعاقب على إتيانه بوصف غير مشروع، وأن لا يوجب هذا الفعل نيل الدرجات العظيمة لاشتماله على وصف غير مشروع فليس ببعيد أن يقال إن ملازمة الارتكاب بالمنهي عنه أبطل أجر الحسنة لكنه سقط الذمة المشغولة بها بوجودها فالسقوط عن الذمة بفعلها وهو نحو من الثواب وإذا عرف الحال في العبادات ففي المعاملات بالطريق الأولى، وما ذكره مطلع الأسرار الإلهية» أن النافي للصحة متحقق في المعاملات وهو النهي، فلا يفقهه هذا العبد، فإن النهي في الشرعيات مطلقاً أو المعاملات فقط مقتض للصحة، فكيف يكون نافياً، ومن ادعى فعليه البيان، فافهم وهو أعلم بالصواب.
[Whether] it is correct to make the second madhhab opposite to it, for no one has gone to negate this fasad even in transactions. If you say: what is meant is fasad bi al-dhat, and the discussion is when the prohibition is on the essence not due to a description as stated in al-Mukhtasar, I say: a beneficial gloss is transmitted here that the prohibition is absolute, whether for its essence or for a description. Also, at-talluq (relation) of prohibition to the essence in shar'iyyat is not valid according to us, so 'ibadat do not enter the subject of the issue, so it is not valid to transmit the second madhhab as opposite to the first here. There is no answer to this investigation except to say: what is meant is that the prohibition qua prohibition without observing a diverting qarina indicates fasad in its essence. In the second madhhab there is no indication except in 'ibadat. As for other shar'iyyat, even if they do not indicate according to us, except that the shar'iyya diverts from it. So reflect, for it is a subject of reflection for us. First, the scholars of the cities in the ages have not ceased to deduce fasad from prohibition absolutely, whether it be worship or transaction. Thus its indication of fasad is agreed upon. And in it, as will come, this is conceded in non-shar'iyyat, but in shar'iyyat it is not conceded. Even if the claimant makes it that it in itself, disregarding external qara'in, indicates fasad. The upshot is that the scholars of the cities used to deduce by the very prohibition fasad in its essence, and they needed for fasad due to others a diverting qarina. So it is for fasad legally. However, the Hanafi argument that it is for essential validity and fasad due to description, if complete, indicates that the requirement of the very prohibition in shar'iyyat is that the shar'iyya is fasad, so reflect. (And) secondly: the wisdom of the prohibitor requires ugliness (qubh) of the prohibited. For the wise only prohibits abomination, so ugliness is inherent in it, and the ugly in the view of the wise does not bring about fruits at all, so fasad necessarily follows. [Objection:] that the wisdom of the prohibitor only requires absolute ugliness, not essential ugliness so as to necessitate essential fasad. And if it is aided that the absolute is diverted to the perfect individual, and perfect ugliness is that which is essential, then [reply:] that is when the indicator is used in a literal sense and it has perfect and imperfect individuals, it is diverted to the perfect. But here prohibition is not a subject for ugliness nor for fasad, but is necessitated by the necessity of the wisdom of the prohibitor. So it is from the category of iqtida' or imperative implication which is subsidiary, and there is no diversion in them to the perfect alone. Rather, it will become clear to you, Allah willing, that the wisdom of the prohibitor which requires the prohibited to be possible indicates the prohibition in shar'iyyat on essential beauty and ugliness due to others. So wait for those who deny indication of fasad absolutely. They said: if prohibition indicated fasad, then an explicit statement of validity would be contradictory, which is false, for we know with certainty that if the Lawgiver said: 'Do not divorce during menses, and if you divorce it takes effect and its rulings apply, and if you do I will punish you,' it would be valid. (The answer is: we deny the implication, for the explicit statement of validity changes it. And you may formulate their argument: if it indicated fasad, then explicit validity would be contradictory to its meaning in the intellect, just as contradiction is contradictory to the meaning of 'lion' in the intellect, while it is not so due to certain knowledge of what was mentioned, but there is reflection on the falsity of the consequent.) Those who restrict to fasad in 'ibadat said: 'ibadat are commanded (ma'mur biha) so they cannot be prohibited (manhiyyah 'anha) due to opposition between them. (The answer: it is permissible for the prohibition to refer to the description, so they are commanded in essence, and the prohibition is due to their inclusion of the description, so there is no opposition because of the difference of the loci of the command and prohibition. This answer is only effective for one who denies that it indicates fasad absolutely. As for one who claims fasad, this only results in fasad being necessary in prohibited 'ibadat, but it does not indicate at all that it is not in transactions. Rather, it is possible that there is another proof there. So reflect, for transactions may also be obligatory, so they are commanded, so they cannot be prohibited like 'ibadat. Thus the proof is rebutted. And it can be repelled that some transactions are not commanded, so it is possible that they are prohibited unlike 'ibadat, for all of them are commanded, so they conflict with prohibition. This is in al-Hashiya. And similar is refuted against the original proof, for some 'ibadat are supererogatory, so they are not commanded unless what is meant by commanded is general. Then he said: with this it is repelled that transactions are permissible (mubah), so they cannot be prohibited due to opposition. And the face of the repelling is not clear except that some of them are haram, so prohibition attaches to them. And in it is apparent reflection. For the claim of the arguer is that prohibition in transactions does not necessitate fasad unlike 'ibadat due to opposition in commandedness and prohibition, and transactions are not commanded, so the speech is directed. So it is established that they are not commanded, but they are absolutely mubah, so prohibition is opposed to them. If it is meant that some of them are not mubah in essence, then fasad would be necessary in them too, which is against the madhhab of the arguer, and the rebuttal is complete. And if it is meant that they are haram due to description, then they would be mubah in essence, so prohibition is opposed to them. And if a distinction is made that permissibility is for essence and prohibition is for description, then it turns against the original proof. The rebuttal must be such that it does not turn at all, so reflect. Then it has become clear to you that the relied-upon answer is to create a difference in locus (muhall). And Ibn al-Humam strengthened the pillars of inference: that the purpose in 'ibadat is reward, so if they are prohibited, their commission becomes a cause of punishment, so the 'ibada becomes devoid of its fruit by the prohibition, thus it cannot be legislated (mashru'ah) at all. As for transactions, there is no problem that the prohibitor says: 'I have made this thing a cause for this, but do not do it, and if you do I will punish you.' The essential legislation is not devoid of its worldly benefit, even if it is a cause of punishment in the Hereafter, like sale: its ruling is ownership, and it is established despite prohibition. 'Ibadat have no worldly fruit; rather their fruit is only reward, and it has been rendered nonexistent by the prohibition, so it is not valid. And 'Matli' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah' in Sharh al-Manar said: what he mentioned regarding 'ibadat is correct, and transactions should also be similar; for the negator of validity is real, and it is the prohibition, and what he mentioned of the example has a qarina that diverts from the requirement of the prohibition. And this slave says: what Ibn al-Humam mentioned is repelled. For suppose the purpose in 'ibadat is reward, but we do not concede that it contradicts the attachment of prohibition which necessitates punishment. For it is possible that one is rewarded and punished for a single act. For since we have permitted that a thing is an 'ibada and legislated in itself and is prohibited and unlegislated due to its description, if the mukallaf performs this act, he deserves to be given the reward of the act itself and to be punished for performing it with an unlegislated description. And that this act does not cause attaining great ranks due to its inclusion of an unlegislated description. So it is not far that it be said that the inevitability of commission of the prohibited invalidated the reward of the good deed, but the liability occupied by it fell due to its existence. So the falling from liability by performing it is a kind of reward. And if this is known regarding 'ibadat, then transactions come first (by primary analogy). And what 'Matli' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah' mentioned that the negator of validity is real in transactions, and it is the prohibition, this slave does not understand. For the prohibition in shar'iyyat absolutely, or transactions only, necessitates validity, so how can it be a negator? And whoever claims (otherwise) must provide proof. So understand, and He knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute on whether a prohibition (nahy) indicates invalidity (fasad). The text begins by addressing a potential objection: if someone claims that the second opinion (that nahy only indicates fasad in 'ibadat) is opposite to the first (that it indicates fasad absolutely), the author argues that no one actually denies fasad entirely, even in transactions. He then clarifies that the discussion concerns prohibitions of the essence (dhat) not due to an external description. A beneficial gloss is cited stating that the prohibition is absolute, whether for essence or description. The author notes that according to the Hanafis, a prohibition cannot attach to the essence in shar'iyyat (religious matters), so 'ibadat are excluded from the subject. Thus the second opinion cannot simply be set as opposite. The true meaning is that the prohibition qua prohibition indicates essential fasad, while the second opinion restricts it to 'ibadat. The author then presents the Hanafi claim that scholars throughout the ages have used prohibition to deduce fasad absolutely, and this is agreed upon (mujma' alayh). However, this agreement only applies to non-shar'iyyat; in shar'iyyat it is contested. The Hanafi evidence is then discussed: the wisdom of the Prohibitor requires ugliness (qubh) in the prohibited, and this necessarily entails fasad. An objection is raised: the wisdom only requires absolute ugliness, not essential ugliness. The response uses the principle that an absolute term diverts to the perfect individual, and perfect ugliness is that which is essential. But this is rejected because prohibition is not put for ugliness or fasad; it is implied by necessity, and there is no diversion in imperative implication. The conclusion is that the wisdom of the prohibitor only indicates that the prohibited is possible, and in shar'iyyat it indicates essential beauty and ugliness due to others. Then the text presents the argument of those who deny indication of fasad absolutely: if nahy indicated fasad, then an explicit statement of validity would be contradictory. But we know that the Lawgiver can say, 'Do not divorce in menses, and if you divorce it takes effect,' which is valid. The answer is that the explicit statement changes the implication. The text then discusses the view that restricts fasad to 'ibadat. The argument is that 'ibadat are commanded, so they cannot be prohibited due to opposition. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition can refer to the description, so the act is commanded in essence and prohibited due to description, thus no opposition due to different loci. This answer only works for a denier of absolute fasad. For the claimant of fasad, it only shows fasad in 'ibadat, not that it is absent in transactions. Transactions may be obligatory or permissible, so they also cannot be prohibited. The discussion becomes intricate. The relied-upon answer is to create a difference in locus (muhall). Ibn al-Humam's argument is then presented: the purpose of 'ibadat is reward; if prohibited, the act causes punishment and is devoid of fruit, so it cannot be legislated. Transactions have worldly benefits, so they can remain valid despite prohibition (e.g., sale during Friday prayer). A counter-argument from 'Matli' al-Asrar' says that the prohibitor of validity is real (the prohibition itself) in both 'ibadat and transactions. The author (as 'this slave') disagrees, stating that the prohibition in shar'iyyat necessitates validity, not invalidity. Thus the passage ends with the author asserting the Hanafi position that nahy does not indicate fasad in shar'iyyat absolutely, and the burden of proof lies on the claimant of fasad.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A demand to abstain from an action, issuing from a higher authority.
الفساد (al-fasad): Invalidity or lack of legal effect in a religious act or transaction.
العبادات (al-'ibadat): Acts of worship prescribed by the Shari'ah, such as prayer and fasting.
المعاملات (al-mu'amalat): Transactions and contracts, such as sale and marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, what is the effect of a prohibition (nahy) on a sale during Friday prayer (a prohibited transaction)?
A) The sale is invalid (fasid) and produces no ownership.
B) The sale is valid but sinful, and ownership transfers.
C) The sale is valid only if the prohibition is on the essence.
D) The sale is valid and not sinful if the prohibition is on the description.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, prohibition does not necessarily invalidate an act; the act's essential validity depends on whether the prohibition targets the essence or a description.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Interpreting Prohibition on Intrinsically Good Acts
Responding to objections regarding prohibition of acts lacking conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a Quranic or hadith prohibition on a good act must be interpreted metaphorically and apply the Hanafi usul principle that reason (al-'aql) takes precedence over an apparent textual meaning (al-naql) in such cases.

Core Idea
A genuine prohibition cannot attach to an act that is intrinsically good—interpretation is required.

This passage tackles a crucial usul problem: how can the hadith 'Leave prayer during your menses' be reconciled with the fact that prayer is a praiseworthy act? The Hanafi school argues that real prohibition (al-nahy al-haqiqi) cannot apply to something inherently good (hasan). Therefore, the apparent prohibition must be interpreted metaphorically. This lesson unpacks the two main Hanafi interpretations and the rational principle behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent conflict
The hadith says 'Leave prayer during your menses.' This seems to prohibit prayer itself. Yet prayer is an act of worship that is intrinsically good. In Hanafi usul, something good cannot be genuinely prohibited. So we must resolve this conflict.
This is not about the technicality of menstruation; it's about the general principle that good acts cannot be haram in themselves.
2
Apply the overriding principle: reason over textual form
The author states that rational judgment (al-'aql) that real prayer cannot be prohibited takes precedence over the apparent textual meaning (al-naql). This is a key Hanafi rule: when reason and a textual form conflict, the text must be interpreted metaphorically.
This is different from other schools that might accept the prohibition as literal and derive fasad (invalidity).
3
Recognise the two metaphorical interpretations proposed
The passage offers two ta'wil (interpretations). First: the prohibition is metaphorical for negation—'no prayer occurs during menses' (i.e., it is not valid). Second: it prohibits the resolve ('azm) to pray, not the actual prayer. Either way, the real act of prayer is not prohibited.
The text says we adopt one of these two, giving precedence to reason over the literal wording.
4
Understand the concept of legal realities (haqa'iq shar'iyya)
A legal reality like prayer consists of specific pillars (arkan) that produce fruits (thamarāt) only when conditions are present. Absence of a condition (like purity) means the fruit is not produced, but the pillars can exist. The prohibition then attaches to the fruitless existence, which is not a real prohibition of the act itself.
This supports the interpretation that the prohibition is not on the act qua act.
5
Analyse the counter-argument and its refutation
Opponents argue that prayer is divided into valid and invalid, so invalid prayer is still prayer and can be prohibited. The Hanafi response: this division is metaphorical, like dividing 'human' into living and dead—a dead body is not truly a human. A thing missing a pillar is not a real instance of that thing.
Thus, prohibition cannot attach to an invalid prayer because it is not the same reality.

الصلاة أيام أقرائها وهذا في معنى النهي وقد تعلّق بالصلاة المقارنة عدم الشرط، وحاصله النقض بالنهي المتعلق بالشيء المقارن عدم الشرط والركن، فلا تنفع المناقشة في هذا المثال الخاص فتدبر (قلنا) مثله (محمول على بيان الانتفاء أيام الإقراء، يعني النهي مجاز عن النفي، فالمعنى ليس تتحقق صلاة في أيام الإقراء، وهذا تصرف في صيغة النهي (أو) قلنا : النهي ( راجع إلى الإيقاع والعزم) عليه (لا) إلى (الفعل) فالمعنى دعي
