باستحالة تعلق النهي الحقيقي بالصلاة الحقيقية في تلك الأيام على النقل الوارد فيه النهي متعلقاً في تلك الأيام فأوّل، بأحد التأويلين فافهم، وقد يجاب بأن المراد بالصلاة الشبيه بها من القيام والقعود والسجود وغير ذلك أفعال حسية لا يقتضي النهي عنها الصحة وهي أمور ممكنة أيضاً، وسيلوح من كلام المصنف ما يدل على الرضا به لكن هذا إنما يتم لو كانت الحائضة الأمية والخرساء لو أنت بهذه الأركان من غير نية وعزم على الصلاة كانت آثمة وما وجد رواية صريحة فيها (فبيع الحر وهي والمضامين) وهي ما كان في صلب الآباء من النطفة (والملاقيح) وهو ما كان في رحم الأم من الحبل (وما أشبه ذلك كبيع الميتة كلها منفيات أي ليست هي بيوعاً، والنهي الوارد بها ليس على الحقيقة، بل مجاز عن النفي أو ليس ركن البيع، وهو مبادلة المال بالمال مفقوداً، ووجود الشيء من غير وجود الركن من المستحيلات التي لا تصلح لتعلق النهي بها فافهم، أتباع الأئمة الثلاثة (قالوا : الصلاة تنقسم إلى صحيحة وفاسدة، والمقسم مشترك في الأقسام، فالصلاة الفاسدة صلاة حقيقية، وقد ورد النهي عنه (قلنا أولاً هذا التقسيم لعله ورد من أمثالكم، فلا حجة فيه، اللهم إلا إذا ثبت الإجماع عليه، وقلنا ثانياً : سلمنا أنه ورد ممن يوثق به للحجية لكنه ليس على الحقيقة بل ذلك كتقسيم الإنسان إلى الحي والميت فهو تقسيم مجازي، وكيف يدعي أحد أن الشيء الذي لا يوجد فيه ركن أو شرط، فرد لهذا الشيء، وهل هذا إلا كما يقال الحجر فرد للحيوان فافهم .
And it is the third article on linguistic principles absolutely, and they are invalid in the view of the Lawgiver, so their purpose is achieved by saying that prohibition necessitates invalidity, and that repelling by interpreting it as metaphorical concerning sensory pillars is not resorted to except by necessity. And their purpose may be established by saying that the legally defined realities are the specific pillars; they may exist without the legal conditions and have been made to produce specific fruits, but not absolutely, rather if they are with specific conditions, the conditions are not for the existence of that reality but for the ordering of the specific fruits. So their existence without those conditions is possible, but those fruits are not then produced, and the prohibition attached to them when conditions are absent is a prohibition of things possible in themselves whose fruits have become impossible to produce, and that is what they mean by 'impossible', and it suffices for their purpose of establishing the inherent invalidity of prohibition. And its solution will come if God wills. Moreover, the three Imams explicitly stated that al-Fatiha is a pillar of prayer, and its omission necessitates invalidity, and they permitted the attachment of prohibition to it. This is explicit that they allow that prayer, if omitted, is prohibited and detached, even though the thing being separate from its part is inherently impossible. So understand. (And) a second objection was raised: the refutation by the like of his saying, peace be upon him, 'Leave prayer during your menses' – narrated by al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud – and 'you leave prayer during your menses,' and this is in the sense of prohibition, and it is attached to prayer accompanied by absence of condition. The gist is the refutation by the prohibition attached to a thing accompanied by absence of condition and pillar, so the discussion in this particular example is of no use, so ponder. (We say): Such a thing (is interpreted as indicating negation during the days of menses, meaning the prohibition is metaphorical for negation, so the meaning is 'prayer does not occur during the days of menses', and this is a transformation in the form of prohibition. (Or) we say: The prohibition refers to the act of performance and resolve, not to the action itself. So the meaning is 'leave the resolve to pray during your menses', for prayer does not occur then, and resolve upon the impossible is possible if one does not believe in its impossibility, and even with it, even if without benefit. And we adopt one of these two metaphorical interpretations (giving precedence to reason which judges the impossibility of attaching a real prohibition to real prayer during those days over the transmitted text which contains the prohibition attached in those days, so we interpret it with one of the two interpretations), so understand. And it may be answered that what is meant by 'prayer' is the similar actions such as standing, bowing, prostrating, etc., sensory actions whose prohibition does not entail validity, and they are also possible things. And from the author's words something will appear that indicates satisfaction with this, but this is only valid if the illiterate and mute menstruating woman, if she performs these pillars without intention and resolve to pray, were sinful, but no explicit report has been found about that. (Then the sale of a free person, and the embryos) which are the sperm in the loins of fathers, (and the foetuses) which are the pregnancy in the mother's womb, (and similar things like the sale of carrion, all are negations, i.e., they are not sales, and the prohibition mentioned with them is not literal, but metaphorical for negation, or the pillar of sale, which is exchange of property for property, is missing, and the existence of a thing without its pillar is one of the impossible things that are not suitable for the attachment of prohibition), so understand. The followers of the three Imams said: Prayer is divided into valid and invalid, and the divider is common among the divisions, so invalid prayer is real prayer, and prohibition has been mentioned regarding it. (We say first: This division perhaps came from your likes, so it is not proof, unless there is consensus upon it. And we say second: Even if we grant that it came from someone trustworthy for proof, it is not literal, but like the division of human into living and dead, it is a metaphorical division. How can anyone claim that a thing which lacks a pillar or condition is an individual of that thing? Is this not like saying a stone is an individual of animal? So understand.)
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion responding to objections against the position that a prohibition attached to an act necessarily entails its invalidity (fasad). The author begins by reaffirming the position that legal realities (haqa'iq shar'iyya) like prayer consist of specific pillars (arkan) that produce specific fruits (thamarāt) only when accompanied by conditions. If conditions are absent, the act is possible in itself but the fruits are not produced; thus the prohibition is on something that is possible but fruitless, which is sufficient to establish inherent invalidity. The author notes that the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that al-Fatiha is a pillar of prayer and its omission invalidates, yet they allow prohibition to attach—meaning they permit the concept of a 'prohibited prayer' despite the impossibility of an act being detached from its part. The passage then addresses a second objection from the hadith 'Leave prayer during your menses,' which seems to prohibit prayer itself. The author responds by interpreting the prohibition metaphorically: either as a negation (the prayer is not valid during menses) or as a prohibition of the resolve/intention to pray, not the actual prayer. Reason dictates that real prayer cannot be prohibited because it is intrinsically good; thus the transmitted text must be interpreted. Alternatively, some argue the prohibition attaches only to the sensory actions (postures) done during menses, which are not the full reality of prayer. The discussion then moves to sales of prohibited items like free persons, embryos, and carrion, which are not valid sales because the pillar of sale (exchange of property) is missing; the prohibition there is metaphorical for negation. Finally, the followers of the three Imams raise an argument that prayer is divided into valid and invalid, and invalid prayer is still real prayer, so prohibition attaches to it. The author refutes this by stating the division is metaphorical—like dividing human into living and dead—because a thing that lacks its essential pillar cannot be considered a true instance of that thing. The commentary stays strictly with the text, explaining each step without importing external rulings. The lesson demonstrates the Hanafi method of interpreting prohibitions by giving priority to rational principles over apparent textual meanings when necessary.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): Prohibition; a demand to refrain from an action. Here it is argued that real prohibition cannot attach to acts that are intrinsically good (like prayer) unless interpreted metaphorically.
الحقيقة الشرعية (al-haqiqa al-shar'iyya): A reality defined by the Lawgiver, consisting of specific pillars intended to produce a specific result (fruit).
الركن (al-rukn): Pillar; an essential part of an act without which the act does not exist. For example, al-Fatiha is a pillar of prayer.
الشرط (al-shart): Condition; something external to the reality that affects its legal validity. Here, absence of condition does not negate existence but affects the fruit.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage; used to reinterpret apparent prohibition to avoid contradiction with rational principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul presented, what is the primary reason that the hadith 'Leave prayer during your menses' must be interpreted metaphorically?
A) The hadith is weak in transmission
B) Reason dictates that real prayer, being a good act, cannot be subject to real prohibition
C) The hadith was abrogated by later verses
D) The prohibition only applies to the intention, not the action

Key Takeaway
When a prohibition appears on an act that is intrinsically good, the Hanafi school gives precedence to reason and interprets the prohibition metaphorically, either as a negation of validity or as a prohibition of resolve.

Wudu is Not Part of the Concept of Prayer – Distinguishing Condition from Essence
Responding to objection that wudu is part of prayer's concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a condition like wudu is not part of the concept (mufhum) of an act, and evaluate the objection that its necessity implies conceptual inclusion.

Core Idea
A condition is for legal existence, not for the essence; the reality of prayer is its form of glorification, independent of conditions.

This passage tackles a fundamental usul question: if an act is invalid without a condition, does that condition become part of the act's very definition? An objector argues that since prayer without wudu is not prayer, wudu must be conceptually included. The Hanafi response unfolds with a careful distinction between the condition itself and the restriction (taqyid) by accompaniment, supported by a deep analogy of form and matter drawn from the realities of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector claims that because prayer without wudu is invalid in the Hanafi school, wudu must be a part of the concept (mufhum) of prayer. Otherwise, the definition of prayer would include invalid instances.
The objection is not attributed to a specific school but is a logical challenge to the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the Hanafi Response – Denial of Necessity
The Hanafi response begins by denying the necessity: a condition is for the legal realization (wujud shar'i) of the named thing, not a part of its reality (haqiqah). Conditions are external prerequisites.
The well-known Hanafi formula: al-taqyid bi-muqaranat al-shart dakhil, la nafs al-shart.
3
Analyze the Form-Matter Analogy
Prayer's reality consists of pillars (matter) that instantiate glorification (form). Conditions are prerequisites for the existence of that form, but are not part of the definition. Just as an animal's life depends on a specific temperament without that temperament being part of the animal's concept.
The analogy shows that what is necessary for existence need not be part of the essence.
4
Distinguish Concept from Reality
Even if conceptualization of prayer depends on restriction by conditions, this does not mean conditions themselves are part of the concept. The concept includes the restriction but not the condition as a separate element.
This refines the earlier distinction: it is the accompaniment (muqarana) that is included, not the condition itself.
5
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
In fiqh, this distinction ensures that missing a condition invalidates the act but does not negate its essential nature as defined by pillars. It also preserves the clear boundary between rukn (pillar) and shart (condition).
If conditions were part of the concept, they would be indistinguishable from pillars, collapsing the taxonomy of legal obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الجزئية ذلك فتدبر
And (it is objected) that it necessitates that wudu be included in the concept of prayer, because prayer without purification is not prayer according to you, so some part of it must be missing, and that is none other than wudu for example. So it necessitates that it is included while it is an external condition; this is contrary to the claim. Likewise in Sharh al-Mukhtasarah, conforming to its text. Then this is also necessary upon them, for valid prayer is only that which is accompanied by purification, so it would necessitate that purification be included in it. So whatever your answer is, it is our answer. And it is answered by denying the necessitation, because the condition is only for the realization of the named thing legally, not that it is part of the reality of the named thing. It is said: If the named thing were merely the pillars themselves without considering an extra matter, it would necessarily be realized when the pillars are realized even if the condition is missing. And if the Lawgiver does not consider this existence, it would necessitate the annihilation of what exists. Its solution will come, God willing, from us. And according to the well-known, it is established that the restriction by the accompaniment of the condition is included, not the condition itself. So prayer, for example, is expression of the specific pillars accompanied by the conditions, while the conditions are external to it, as in the valid prayer according to you. It is said: The intended meaning of the necessitation is that it necessitates that it be a part of the concept of prayer, not that it be a part of its reality. And by 'part of the concept' is meant that the conceptualization of a thing depends on conceptualizing that part, such that it is part of its definition. For example, the concept of sight is part of the concept of blindness, but it is not part of its reality such that its indication of it is implicative. There is no doubt about this necessity, because if the pillars were not conceptualized as restricted by the accompaniment of the conditions, prayer would not be distinguished from what is not prayer, i.e., the pillars not accompanied by them. I say, firstly: The dependency — i.e., the dependency of the conceptualization of prayer on wudu, etc., such that it enters into its definition — is denied. Its verification is that prayer, for example, is expression of these pillars, but not absolutely; rather, in such a way that they are an instantiation of the glorification of God Almighty. This glorification is like the form and species of the reality of prayer, and the pillars are like its matter. So when the pillars are found and become an instantiation of glorification, the reality of prayer exists in actuality like other realities. But the conditions are things on which the existence of this glorification depends. So by the absence of these conditions, what is like the form is annihilated, and thus the reality is annihilated. And it does not necessitate the dependency of its conceptualization on the conceptualization of the conditions, nor their inclusion in the definition. Just as the life of an animal and the existence of its specific form is dependent on and conditioned by a particular temperament, but it does not necessitate the inclusion of that temperament in its reality or in its concept. Thus, what was said — that if the restriction were not included, then prayer without wudu would be prayer, otherwise it would necessitate the annihilation of what exists, and thus the dependency of conceptualization would be necessitated — is repelled. And what we have mentioned is supported by what is in the Hashiyah: that those who unveil the realities and forms of acts of worship distinguish between the sound, accepted one and others without regard to the condition, and they say of the accepted ones: they are spirits in the world of subtleties. The intended meaning of 'unveilers' is the noble Sufis, for they say that acts of worship have forms in the intermediate world, as witnessed by texts on the weighing of deeds and texts on the guarding of deeds, as reported in the authentic hadith that Surat al-Mulk guards its reader in the Hereafter, and the Noble Qur'an intercedes, etc. The ratio of the sound one to the invalid one is like the ratio of the living to the dead in our world. This is the most balanced proof that prayer has something analogous to the spirit for the body, and the conditions are only conditions for its existence. So understand. And I say, secondly: Even if the necessitation is granted, the invalidity of the consequent is denied, for it does not necessitate the lack of distinction between the pillar and the condition. Rather, the impossibility is in the necessitation of partiality. So reflect.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute regarding whether a condition (shart) like wudu is part of the concept (mufhum) of an act of worship like prayer. The objector argues: since prayer without wudu is not valid in the Hanafi school, wudu must be part of what constitutes prayer. The text responds by denying the necessity: the condition is only for the legal realization of the name (musamma), not part of its reality (haqiqah). The well-known Hanafi position distinguishes between the condition itself and the restriction (taqyid) by accompaniment; the latter is included in the concept but the condition is external. This is clarified through the analogy of form and matter: prayer's reality consists of pillars (matter) that instantiate glorification (form). Conditions are prerequisites for the existence of that form, not parts of the definition. The text supports this with Sufi insight: the sound prayer has a spiritual form distinct from its conditions. The second response adds that even if conceptual dependency were granted, it does not blur the distinction between rukn (pillar) and shart (condition). This is a standard Hanafi position in usul al-fiqh rejecting the inclusion of external conditions in the essence of acts.

Key Terms
وضوء (wudu): Ablution; in this context, a condition (shart) for prayer's legal realization.
مفهوم (mufhum): Concept; the mental understanding of a thing that distinguishes it from others.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Reality; the true essence of a thing as it is in itself.
شرط (shart): Condition; something external whose presence is necessary for the legal existence of an act.
ركن (rukn): Pillar; an essential constitutive part of an act without which it does not exist.
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction; the act of qualifying a thing by an accompaniment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because wudu is a pillar (rukn) of prayer.
B) Because the condition is only for legal realization, not for the essence.
C) Because prayer with wudu is still invalid.
D) Because wudu is a part of the reality (haqiqah) but not the concept.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a condition (shart) is external to the concept of an act; it is only necessary for the legal realization of the act's essence, not a part of that essence.

The Principle of Prohibition Implying Continuity and Generality
Usul rule: prohibition implies continuity and generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that a prohibition implies immediate cessation (fawr) and continuous/general applicability (dawam/umum) and distinguish when a qualification (takhsis) restricts its scope.

Core Idea
A prohibition commands immediate and perpetual restraint from an act for all individuals, unless a clear qualification limits its reach.

This passage lays out a foundational usul rule: what does a divine or prophetic prohibition actually mean? The Hanafi school, along with the majority of usul scholars, holds that a prohibition implies not just that the act is forbidden now, but that it is forbidden continuously for all time and for all people, unless something in the text restricts it. This principle shapes how Hanafi jurists derive rulings from prohibitory texts. The passage also addresses objections and clarifies the concept of continuity in qualified prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule's claim
What does the majority hold regarding prohibition? Note the key terms: fawr, dawam, umum.
Focus on the phrase 'النهي يقتضي الدوام والعموم'.
2
Trace the evidence
Why do they say the apparent meaning (al-mutabadir) is continuity? The practice of scholars using prohibitions across different times without seeking context shows this.
The evidence is istiqra (inductive survey) of scholarly usage.
3
Understand rebuttal of objections
How does the author respond to the objection about heedlessness and the menstruating woman?
Restraint is required only when conscious; qualified prohibitions are continuous within their qualification.
4
Grasp thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference does this rule make in istinbat?
It shifts the burden of restriction onto those who would narrow a prohibition.
5
Recognize nuance
Continuity is relative: absolute prohibitions apply lifelong; qualified prohibitions apply only during the qualifying condition.
The author says 'مقيد عم أوقات القيد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (النهي يقتضي الدوام والعموم (عند الأكثر من أهل الأصول وأهل العربية (فهو للفور) بخلاف الأمر (وقيل : كالأمر في عدم اقتضائه الدوام، بل العموم أيضاً (وفي المحصول أنه المختار، وفي الحاصل أنه الحق لنا استدلال العلماء سلفاً وخلفاً بالنهي على تحريم الفعل (مع اختلاف الأوقات من غير انتظار إلى قرينة دالة على الدوام (فدل) هذا الاستدلال منهم على أن المتبادر منه نفي الحقيقة للفعل أو الفرد المنتشر (وهو) إنما يكون بالانتفاء دائماً لجميع الأفراد عرفاً ولغة فالنفي له حقيقة فلا يرد أنه يستعمل لكل منهما من الدوام وغيره، فلا يكون مشتركاً لفظياً فيهما ولا حقيقة ولا مجازاً، لأن الكل خلاف الأصل، بل يكون للقدر المشترك بينهما، وجه الدفع ظاهر، فإن خلاف الأصل قد يصار إليه الدليل، وههنا قد دل الدليل على تبادر أحدهما فيكون حقيقة فيه ومجازاً في الآخر (لا يقال الكف لا يتأتى مع الدوام فإنه لا يتأتى حال الغفلة، فلا يصلح واجباً على الدوام، وإلا لزم العصيان (لأن الاقتضاء) والتكليف (ما دام الشعور) وعنده يجب الكف دائماً ولا فساد فيه وقد مر من قبل (قالوا: نهي الحائض لا يدوم فلا يلزمه الدوام (قلنا) إنه (مقيد عم أوقات القيد) ومرادنا من الدوام الدوام مدة العمر في المطلق ومدة القيد في المقيد فافهم.
Issue: The prohibition implies continuity and generality. According to most of the experts in usul and Arabic, it is for immediate cessation, unlike the command. And it is said: like the command in not implying continuity, but also not generality. In al-Mahsul, it is stated that this is the chosen view. In al-Hasil, it is stated that it is the truth. For us, the evidence is that scholars, both earlier and later, have used a prohibition to prove the prohibition of an act across different times without waiting for a context indicating continuity. So this usage by them indicates that the apparent meaning (al-mutabadir) from a prohibition is the negation of the reality of the act or of its widespread individual instances. That negation is only realized by it being perpetually absent for all individuals, customarily and linguistically. So the negation (al-nafy) has a real meaning, so it is not said that it is used for each of the two (continuity and non-continuity) or that it is homonymous (mushtarak lafzi) between them, nor that it is used in a real and metaphorical sense, because all of that is contrary to the original principle. Rather, it is for the common denominator between them; the reason for the objection is clear. However, the original principle may be set aside due to a proof, and here the proof indicates that one of the two (the immediate cessation) is the apparent meaning, so the term is real for that and metaphorical for the other. It cannot be said that restraint (al-kaf) cannot be perpetual because it is not possible during a state of heedlessness, so it cannot be obligatory continuously, otherwise it would entail sin. Because the requirement (al-iqtida') and the obligation (al-taklif) are as long as there is consciousness. According to the one who holds this, the required restraint must be perpetual always, and there is no problem in that, as has been previously mentioned. They said: the prohibition on the menstruating woman does not continue, so it does not necessitate continuity. We say: it is qualified by the times of the qualification. Our intended meaning of continuity is continuity for the duration of life in the absolute case, and continuity for the duration of the qualification in the qualified case. So understand.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule defining the legal implications of a prohibition (nahy). It begins by stating that the majority of usul scholars and Arabic linguists hold that a prohibition indicates immediate cessation (al-fawr) and does not imply continuity, contrary to the command. However, some say it is like the command in not implying continuity, but it does imply generality. The author then presents the Hanafi argument: the practice of scholars across generations, who used prohibitions to prove the prohibition of an act at different times without needing contextual evidence for continuity, shows that the apparent meaning of a prohibition is the negation of the act's reality and its universal applicability. This prohibition is understood to be continuous because the negation must be perpetual for all instances. The author addresses an objection: if a prohibition requires continuous restraint, it would be impossible during times of heedlessness, thus entailing sin. The response is that the obligation and requirement are conditional upon consciousness; as long as one is conscious, they must refrain. Another objection is raised: the prohibition on a menstruating woman is not continuous (since it is specific to the state of menstruation), so it does not imply general continuity. The author replies that such a prohibition is qualified by its specific occasion; their definition of 'continuity' means throughout the duration of the qualification, not necessarily the entire life. The passage concludes with 'فافهم' (so understand), emphasizing the point.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A command to refrain from an act; it implies immediate and continuous cessation according to the majority.
الدوام (al-dawām): Continuity over time; in the context of nahy, it means the prohibition applies continuously throughout the relevant period (e.g., lifetime for absolute prohibitions, or duration of qualification for qualified ones).
الفور (al-fawr): Immediate cessation; the requirement to stop the act as soon as the prohibition is known.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what does a prohibition (nahy) primarily indicate?
A) That the act is disliked (makruh).
B) Immediate cessation and continuous negative applicability across all times and individuals unless qualified.
C) That the act is only prohibited at the moment of revelation.
D) That it is similar to a command in implying deliberation (tartb).

Key Takeaway
A prohibition in the Quran or Sunnah is understood by the Hanafi school as requiring immediate, continuous, and universal abandonment of the act, unless a clear restriction is provided.

Definition of Ibarah (Explicit Indication) in Hanafi Usul
Defining the first type of verbal indication: al-ibarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-ibarah and distinguish it from al-isharah, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether secondary intention is included.

Core Idea
Al-ibarah is the direct indication of wording, including intended meanings even if secondary—disputed by Sadr al-Shari'ah.

This lesson introduces the first of four types of verbal indication (dalalat al-lafz) in Hanafi usul: al-ibarah. You will learn its precise definition, its conditions, and how it differs from al-isharah. A classic example from the Quran clarifies the concept, and a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement with Sadr al-Shari'ah deepens your understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of verbal indication
The passage opens by stating that Hanafi usul recognizes four types. While they are not all named here, al-ibarah is the first. Recognizing this taxonomy helps contextualize the definition.
2
Define al-ibarah with its core conditions
Al-ibarah is an indication established through the wording itself (al-nazm), without an intermediary understood meaning (like in dalalah) and without correction of speech (like in iqtida'). It includes both primary and secondary intentions of the speaker.
3
Analyze the Quranic example (2:275)
The verse 'And Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury' is presented as an example. According to the majority, the verse indicates three things by ibarah: permissibility (secondary intention), prohibition (secondary intention), and the distinction between them (primary intention). Trace which meaning is intended how.
4
Examine the dispute with Sadr al-Shari'ah
Sadr al-Shari'ah restricted ibarah to primary intention only, making the permissibility and prohibition fall under isharah. The passage rejects this as a change of terminology without benefit. Understand the basis of each view.
5
Distinguish ibarah from isharah
Isharah is a required indication (dalalah iltizamiyyah) not intended at all. Unlike ibarah, isharah is weaker and minds vary in grasping it. Learning this contrast solidifies the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل (دلالة اللفظ عندنا أربع وأما من عدانا فيزيد عليه (منها العبارة وهو ما ثبت أي دلالة ثبتت وتحققت (بالنظم) بأن يدل هو بنفسه لا بواسطة معنى مفهوم كما في الدلالة، ولا بواسطة تصحيح الكلام كما في الاقتضاء (ولو التزاماً) أي ولو كانت التزامية (مقصوداً به، ولو كان القصد (تبعاً) احتراز عن الإشارة (كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَحَلَّ اللهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبوا ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٧٥] الآية فالحل والحرمة والتفرقة اللازمة لهما كلها بالعبارة) لأن الأولين مقصودان تبعاً، والتفرقة مقصودة بالذات لكون الآية رداً لتسويتهم بينهما، فالعبارة يعتبر فيها السوق للمعنى المفهوم في الجملة بالذات أو بالتبع، صرح به صاحب الكشف ونقله عن الإمام صدر الإسلام أيضاً، وعزي إلى الإمام شمس الأئمة، وفيه خلاف صدر الشريعة حيث شرط فيها السوق بالذات حتى حكم على الدلالة على حل البيع وحرمة الربا أنها إشارة ورد بأن تغيير الاصطلاح من غير فائدة في قوّة الخطأ عند المحصلين ومنها الإشارة، وهي دلالة التزامية لا تقصد أصلا لا بالذات ولا بالتبع ولا بد من تقييد زائد هو أن لا تكون لتصحيح الكلام ليخرج الاقتضاء والأذهان متفاوتة في فهمها لكونها بعلاقة اللزوم، وهو قد يكوم جلياً، فدلالته جلية، وقد
Section: The indications of wording according to us are four, and according to those other than us, they add more. Among them is al-ibarah, which is what is established—that is, an indication that is confirmed and realized through the wording itself (al-nazm), by indicating by itself not through an understood meaning as in al-dalalah, nor through correction of speech as in al-iqtida', even if it is a required (ilitzami) meaning, and even if the intention is subsidiary (taba'an), as a safeguard against al-isharah. For example, the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury' (al-Baqara 2:275). So the permissibility, prohibition, and the necessary distinction between them are all by ibarah, because the first two are intended secondarily, and the distinction is intended primarily since the verse is a response to their equating them. So ibarah considers the conveyance of the understood meaning in the sentence, either primarily or secondarily. Al-Kashf stated this and transmitted it from Imam Sadr al-Islam, and it is attributed to Imam Shams al-A'imma. There is a disagreement with Sadr al-Shari'ah, who stipulated that it must be primarily intended, so he ruled that the indication of the permissibility of sale and prohibition of usury is isharah. The response is that changing the terminology without benefit is close to error according to the investigators. One of them is al-isharah, which is a required indication that is not intended at all, neither primarily nor secondarily, and it requires an additional qualification that it should not be for correction of speech to exclude iqtida', and minds vary in understanding it because it is based on a relationship of entailment. It may be clear, so its indication is clear.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage opens a section on the four types of verbal indication (dalalat al-lafz) in Hanafi usul, noting that other schools add more. It begins by defining the first type: al-ibarah (explicit indication). Al-ibarah is the indication that is established directly through the wording itself (al-nazm), without needing an intermediary understood meaning (like in dalalah) or correction of speech (like in iqtida'). It includes even illizami (implied) meanings as long as they are intended, either primarily or secondarily. The key example is from Quran 2:275: 'And Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury.' According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Kashf, Imam Sadr al-Islam, Imam Shams al-A'imma), the verse indicates three things by ibarah: the permissibility of trade (intended secondarily), the prohibition of usury (intended secondarily), and the distinction between them (intended primarily, because the verse is refuting those who equated them). However, Sadr al-Shari'ah disagreed, arguing that ibarah requires the meaning to be primarily intended; thus he considered the permissibility and prohibition to be indicated by isharah (indirect indication). The passage replies that changing established terminology without benefit is close to error. It then introduces the second type, al-isharah, which is a required indication (dalalah iltizamiyyah) that is not intended at all, either primarily or secondarily. It also requires the additional condition that it should not be for correction of speech (to exclude iqtida'). People's minds vary in understanding isharah because it depends on a relationship of entailment; it may be clear (jali) so its indication is clear.

Key Terms
العبارة (al-ʿibārah): The direct indication of wording (lafz) through its own composition (nazm) conveying an intended meaning, whether primarily or secondarily.
الإشارة (al-ishārah): An implied indication (dalalah iltizamiyyah) that is not intended at all, often understood through a necessary relation (talazum) with the explicit meaning.
النظم (al-nazm): The composition or wording itself, as opposed to the meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view in this passage, which of the following is indicated by ibarah in the verse 'And Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury'?
A) Only the permissibility of trade.
B) Only the prohibition of usury.
C) The permissibility of trade, prohibition of usury, and the distinction between them.
D) The prohibition of trade.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, ibarah includes meanings intended secondarily; a stricter view that excludes them is rejected by the majority.

Prohibition Implies Continuity and Generality
Defining the implication of prohibition in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that prohibition (nahy) implies continuity (dawam) and generality (umum), and apply it to derive the ruling of perpetual abstention from a prohibited act.

Core Idea
Prohibition demands perpetual and universal abstention — nahy implies dawam wa umum.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author establishes a foundational usul principle: when the Lawgiver prohibits an act, the default understanding is that the prohibition is binding at all times and in all forms. This distinguishes nahy from amr (command) and carries significant implications for deriving legal rulings. We will explore the majority view, the opposing argument, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
State the rule: al-nahy yqtadi al-dawam wa al-umum. Define each term within the Hanafi usul framework.
Dawam refers to the continuity of abstention for the entire duration of the restriction (whether absolute or conditional).
2
Analyze the majority proof
The author points to the consistent practice of scholars who deduce prohibition from a nahy without waiting for a contextual indicator. This shows the primary understanding of nahy is perpetual negation.
3
Examine the opposing view (qila)
Some scholars analogize nahy to amr, claiming it does not necessitate continuity. Identify the weakness: nahy has its own distinct implication.
The opposing view is a minority position.
4
Address objections
Two objections are raised: (1) impossibility of perpetual abstention due to heedlessness, and (2) the temporary nature of prohibition for menstruating women. See how the Hanafi replies clarify the rule.
The replies affirm the principle by tying obligation to consciousness and restricting continuity to the duration of the qualification.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical difference: under Hanafi usul, a general prohibition covers all times and forms unless specified otherwise; the opposing view allows exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلقاً
مسألة
(النهي يقتضي الدوام والعموم (عند الأكثر من أهل الأصول وأهل العربية (فهو للفور) بخلاف الأمر (وقيل : كالأمر في عدم اقتضائه الدوام، بل العموم أيضاً (وفي المحصول أنه المختار، وفي الحاصل أنه الحق لنا استدلال العلماء سلفاً وخلفاً بالنهي على تحريم الفعل (مع اختلاف الأوقات من غير انتظار إلى قرينة دالة على الدوام (فدل) هذا الاستدلال منهم على أن المتبادر منه نفي الحقيقة للفعل أو الفرد المنتشر (وهو) إنما يكون بالانتفاء دائماً لجميع الأفراد عرفاً ولغة فالنفي له حقيقة فلا يرد أنه يستعمل لكل منهما من الدوام وغيره، فلا يكون مشتركاً لفظياً فيهما ولا حقيقة ولا مجازاً، لأن الكل خلاف الأصل، بل يكون للقدر المشترك بينهما، وجه الدفع ظاهر، فإن خلاف الأصل قد يصار إليه الدليل، وههنا قد دل الدليل على تبادر أحدهما فيكون حقيقة فيه ومجازاً في الآخر (لا يقال الكف لا يتأتى مع الدوام فإنه لا يتأتى حال الغفلة، فلا يصلح واجباً على الدوام، وإلا لزم العصيان (لأن الاقتضاء) والتكليف (ما دام الشعور) وعنده يجب الكف دائماً ولا فساد فيه وقد مر من قبل (قالوا: نهي الحائض لا يدوم فلا يلزمه الدوام (قلنا) إنه (مقيد عم أوقات القيد) ومرادنا من الدوام الدوام مدة العمر في المطلق ومدة القيد في المقيد فافهم.
Absolutely. Issue: The prohibition necessitates continuity and generality according to most of the scholars of usul and Arabic scholars. So it is for immediate cessation (al-fawr), unlike the command. And it was said: it is like the command in that it does not necessitate continuity, but also generality. And in al-Mahsul it is the chosen view, and in al-Hasil it is the truth. Our proof: the scholars, early and late, deduced from the prohibition the impermissibility of the act across different times without awaiting a contextual indicator indicating continuity. So this deduction of theirs indicates that the primary understanding from it is the negation of the reality of the act or the distributed individual, and that is only by perpetual negation for all individuals customarily and linguistically. So negation has a reality. So it cannot be said that it is used for both continuity and its opposite, so it would be a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) in both, neither literal nor metaphorical, because all that is contrary to the principle. Rather it is for the common meaning between them. The refutation is apparent, for what is contrary to principle may be adopted by evidence, and here evidence indicates the primacy of one, so it is literal in it and metaphorical in the other. It cannot be said that abstention (kaff) cannot occur with continuity, for it cannot occur during heedlessness, so it cannot be obligatory perpetually, otherwise disobedience would follow. Because the requirement and obligation are while there is consciousness, and according to him abstention is perpetually obligatory and there is no corruption in it, and it has passed before. They said: the prohibition of the menstruating woman is not perpetual, so perpetual obligation is not necessitated. We say: it is restricted to the times of the restriction, and our intent by continuity is continuity for the duration of life in unqualified contexts and for the duration of the restriction in restricted contexts. So understand.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage establishes a key usul principle: prohibition (nahy) inherently implies both continuity (dawam) and generality (umum) — meaning the prohibited act must be avoided at all times and in all forms. The author first states the majority view that nahy demands immediate cessation (fawr), unlike the command (amr) which does not demand immediacy. Some scholars liken nahy to amr in that it does not necessitate continuity, but the author defends the majority by pointing to the practice of scholars who deduce prohibition across different times without needing a separate indicator for continuity. The passage then addresses an objection: how can one abstain perpetually when heedlessness may occur? The reply is that the obligation applies only when one is conscious. Another objection regarding the menstruating woman (whose prohibition of prayer is temporary) is answered by noting that the prohibition is restricted to the time of menstruation; continuity here means for the duration of the restriction. Thus, the default understanding of nahy is perpetual and universal abstention.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A demand to abstain from an act from a superior position, implying perpetual avoidance.
الدوام (al-dawam): Continuity; the obligation of abstention persists for all times within the duration of the restriction or absolutely.
العموم (al-umum): Generality; the prohibition applies to all individuals and instances of the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented, what does a prohibition (nahy) primarily imply?
A) It implies only immediate cessation without continuity.
B) It implies both continuity and generality of abstention.
C) It implies continuity but not generality.
D) It implies neither continuity nor generality, like a command.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that prohibition implies continuity and generality is foundational for understanding the scope of all divine prohibitions in Islamic law.

Understanding Dalala al-Fahwa: Linguistic Implication in Hanafi Usul
Defining dalala al-fahwa and proving Hanafi stance on kaffarah for eating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dalala al-fahwa, identify its conditions, and apply it to derive the Hanafi ruling on kaffarah for intentional eating in Ramadan.

Core Idea
Dalala al-fahwa extends a ruling to an unspoken case if the linguistic manat is understood, no priority required.

This lesson introduces dalala al-fahwa, a key Hanafi method of deriving rulings from the Quran and Sunnah. Through the example of kaffarah for eating in Ramadan, you will see how the Hanafi school differs from the Shafi'i school. We will unpack the definition, conditions, and application of this important usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and key terms
Dalala al-fahwa is the establishment of the ruling of the spoken text (mantooq) for the unspoken (maskut) by understanding the manat (underlying cause) linguistically. Key terms: mantooq, maskut, manat, dalala, fahwa.
The manat must be understood by any competent speaker of the language.
2
Understand the condition: linguistically understood manat
The indication is valid only if the manat is clearly understood from the language itself. The author rebuts the objection that not everyone perceives it, clarifying that what is claimed is the understanding of the manat, not the ruling.
The example of the Bedouin's question: the manat is 'complete violation of the fast' – this is linguistically evident.
3
Analyze the example of the Bedouin's question
The Bedouin said 'I am destroyed, I had intercourse with my wife in Ramadan.' The Prophet prescribed kaffarah. The Hanafi position: the manat is the complete nullification of the fast, not the specific act. Eating also completes nullification, so kaffarah is wajib by dalala al-fahwa.
This shows that priority (اولوية) is not required; the ruling extends equally.
4
Compare with Shafi'i: priority vs no priority
Imam al-Shafi'i requires the unspoken case to be more deserving (priority) in the manat. Hanafis reject this condition. Practical difference: Hanafis obligate kaffarah for intentional eating; Shafi'is do not. Also, Hanafis obligate kaffarah on the woman (her enabling is an act).
Check the thamarah al-khilaf: the same manat leads to same ruling in Hanafi, but Shafi'i requires a stronger connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شيبة عن أم المؤمنين عائشة الصديقة رضي الله عنها قالت : قد كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم يدركه الفجر في رمضان وهو جنب من أهله ثم يغتسل ويصوم، ومنها ما أخرج مالك وابن أبي شيبة والشيخان وأبو داود والترمذي والنسائي عن أم المؤمنين أم سلمة الله رضي عنها أنها سئلت عن الرجل يصبح جنباً ويصوم فقالت: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم يصبح جنباً من جماع غير احتلام في رمضان ثم يصوم. ومنها ما أخرج مالك والشافعي ومسلم وأبو داود والنسائي عن أم المؤمنين عائشة الصديقة أن رجلاً قال : يا رسول الله إني أصبح جنباً وأنا أريد ، الصيام ، فقال النبي يا الله وأنا أصبح جنباً ، وأنا أريد الصيام فأغتسل وأصوم ذلك اليوم فقال : الرجل إنك لست مثلنا قد غفر الله لك ما تقدم من ذنبك وما تأخر، فغضب وقال : إني لأرجو أن أكون أخشاكم الله وأعلمكم بما أتبع ومنها الدلالة والفحوى وهو ثبوت حكم المنطوق للمسكوت بل الدلالة على هذا الثبوت (لفهم المناط) للحكم (لغة) بأن يفهم كل من يعرف اللغة على ما صرح به صاحب الكشف وصدر الشريعة، واعترض صاحب «التلويح بأن أكثر الدلالات مما لم يتفطن لها بعض من لهم اليد الطولى في معرفة اللغة، كالإمام الشافعي لم يفهم وجوب الكفارة بالأكل، ومنشأ هذا الإيراد عدم التدبر في الكلام، فإنه لم يدع انفهام حكم المسكوت لكل بل انفهام المناط، وإنما يختلف في حكم المسكوت لخفاء تحقق هذا المناط المفهوم لغة فيه وفي المثال المضروب يفهم كل من يعرف اللغة أن مناط سؤال الأعرابي وجوابه عليه عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام هو الجناية الكاملة على الصوم، لا نفس القرنية مع الأهل فزعم الشافعي أن الجناية الكاملة هي الإفطار بالوقاع فقط لا غير وعندنا مطلق الإفطار فافهم (كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا تَقُل لَّهُمَا أُنّ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٢٣] فإن اللفظ لتحريم التأفيف) عبارة (ويفهم منه تحريم (الضرب لأجل أن مناط النهي عنه هو الإيذاء، وهذا مفهوم لغة، فكان هذا منهياً عنه ومن جزئياته الضرب فيكون منهياً أيضاً ولا يجب) في الدلالة (أولوية المسكوت في تحقق المناط فيه كما نقل عن الشافعي) فإنا نعلم قطعاً أنه ربما يفهم الحكم في المسكوت مع عدم الأولوية لفهم المناط لغة وإهدار هذا النحو من الدلالة غير لائق، اللهم إلا أن تجدد اصطلاح كما أشار إليه بقوله (وقيل: إنه تنبيه بالأدنى) في المناط على الأعلى فيه، فحينئذ خرج ما فيه المساواة، لكن لا بد من اعتبار قسم آخر سوى الأربعة كما قيل الأول فحوى الخطاب، وما يفهم بالمساواة لحن الخطاب، والمشهور عندهم، أنهما مترادفان (ولهذا) أي ولأنه لا يجب الأولوية في المسكوت أثبتنا الكفارة بعمد الأكل أي بالأكل في نهار شهر رمضان عمداً (كالجماع) الذي ورد فيه إيجاب الكفارة لتبادر أن مناطها التفويت للصوم فإنه سأل الأعرابي وقال : هلكت وأهلكت واقعت أهلي في نهار رمضان، فرتب عليه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام الكفارة وظاهر أنه إنما سأل لكونه جانياً على الصوم جناية كاملة وهذه الجناية لا دخل فيها، لكون الموطوءة أهلاً أو وطئها حلالاً، أي غير زنا وإنما الجناية فيه للتفويت لا غير وهذا ظاهر جداً وهو في الجماع والأكل سواء والجناية بهما على الصوم كاملة فافهم. ومن العجب ما حكي عن الشافعي في قول أنها لا تجب على المرأة مع أن الجناية من كل منهما كاملة وما قيل في توجيهه أن ليس من المرأة فعل، وإنما هي محل لفعل الرجل، فأوهن من بيت العنكبوت، لأن تمكينها للوطء فعل قطعاً فافهم
From [the narrations:] Ibn Abi Shayba from the Mother of the Believers Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, she said: 'The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, would be overtaken by dawn in Ramadan while he was junub from his family, then he would bathe and fast.' And from them [the narrations] is what Malik, Ibn Abi Shayba, the two Shaykhs, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and al-Nasa'i narrated from the Mother of the Believers Umm Salama, may Allah be pleased with her, that she was asked about a man who becomes junub in the morning and fasts. She said: 'The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and his Companions would become junub from intercourse, not from wet dream, in Ramadan, then fast.' And from them is what Malik, al-Shafi'i, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i narrated from Aisha that a man said: 'O Messenger of Allah, I become junub in the morning and I intend to fast.' The Prophet said: 'And I become junub in the morning and I intend to fast, so I bathe and fast that day.' The man said: 'You are not like us; Allah has forgiven you your past and future sins.' He became angry and said: 'I hope that I am the most fearful of Allah among you and the most knowing of what I follow.' And from them [the types] is the indication of the gist (dalala wa al-fahwa), which is the establishment of the ruling of the spoken [text] for the unspoken, rather the indication of this establishment (to understand the manat) of the ruling (linguistically) such that every knower of the language understands it, as stated by the author of al-Kashf and Sadr al-Shariah. The author of al-Talwih objected that most indications are not perceived by some who have mastery in language, like Imam al-Shafi'i who did not understand the obligation of kaffarah for eating. The source of this objection is lack of deliberation in speech, because he did not claim that everyone understands the ruling of the unspoken, rather the understanding of the manat. The disagreement about the ruling of the unspoken is due to the concealment of the realization of this linguistically understood manat in it. In the example given, every knower of the language understands that the manat of the Bedouin's question and the Prophet's answer is the complete violation of the fast, not merely intercourse with the family. Al-Shafi'i claimed that the complete violation is only breaking the fast by intercourse, not other acts. According to us, it is any breaking of the fast. So understand. Like His saying, the Exalted: 'So do not say to them 'uff'' (17:23), for the wording prohibits 'uff', and it is understood from it the prohibition of beating because the manat of the prohibition is causing harm, and this is linguistically understood. So beating is prohibited as it is a part of it. And it is not required in this indication that the unspoken [case] have priority in the realization of the manat, as narrated from al-Shafi'i. We know for certain that the ruling may be understood in the unspoken even without priority, due to understanding the manat linguistically. To disregard this type of indication is not fitting, unless a new convention is established, as he indicated by his saying: 'It is said that it is a notification by the lower [case] about the higher in the manat.' Then what is equal [in the manat] is excluded. But it is necessary to consider another category besides the four, as it is said: the first is fahwa al-khitab, and what is understood by equality is lahn al-khitab. The well-known [view] among them is that they are synonymous. For this reason, i.e., because priority in the unspoken is not required, we established the kaffarah for intentional eating, that is, eating deliberately in the day of Ramadan, like intercourse which has been reported to necessitate kaffarah, because it is understood that its manat is the nullification of the fast. The Bedouin said: 'I am destroyed, I have destroyed myself; I had intercourse with my wife in Ramadan.' So the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, prescribed the kaffarah. It is apparent that he asked because he had committed a complete violation of the fast, and this violation does not involve whether the woman was his wife or if the intercourse was lawful. The violation is only for the nullification, nothing else. This is very clear, and it is the same for intercourse and eating; the violation of the fast is complete in both. So understand. It is strange what is narrated from al-Shafi'i that it is not obligatory on the woman, even though the violation is complete from each. The justification given that the woman does not perform an action, but rather is the locus of the man's action, is weaker than a spider's web, because her enabling of intercourse is certainly an action. So understand.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the concept of dalala al-fahwa (implication of the gist), a type of linguistic indication in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by citing several hadith to establish that a person can be in a state of janabah at dawn and still fast—these serve as examples of textual proof (dalil naqli). He then explains that dalala al-fahwa is the understanding that the ruling of the explicitly mentioned case (mantooq) also applies to the unmentioned case (maskut) due to a common underlying cause (manat) that is linguistically evident. He responds to an objection by the commentator of al-Talwih, who argued that many such indications are not grasped even by experts. The author clarifies that the claim is not that everyone understands the ruling, but that everyone understands the manat linguistically; disagreement arises when the manat is hidden. He gives the example of the Bedouin's question about intercourse in Ramadan: the manat is 'complete violation of the fast' (jinaaya kaamila), which is linguistically understood. Al-Shafi'i limited this to intercourse, while the Hanafis include any act that breaks the fast, such as eating, and thus they also require kaffarah for intentional eating. The author discusses the verse 'Do not say to them 'uff'' (17:23), from which the prohibition of beating is derived via the manat of 'causing harm'. He rejects the condition that the unspoken case must have priority in the manat, citing that the Hanafi school does not require priority; hence kaffarah is wajib for eating just as for intercourse. He also refutes the Shafi'i view that kaffarah is not obligatory on the woman, arguing that her enabling is an act. The passage thus defends the Hanafi position on this usul principle.

Key Terms
الدلالة والفحوى (al-dalala wa al-fahwa): A type of linguistic implication where the ruling of the stated case is understood to apply to an unstated case because the underlying reason (manat) is linguistically evident.
المناط (al-manat): The underlying cause or effective reason that connects the ruling to the case, understood linguistically in dalala al-fahwa.
فحوى الخطاب (fahwa al-khitab): Synonymous with الدلالة والفحوى, but sometimes distinguished as the implication where the unspoken case is more deserving (a fortiori).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position on dalala al-fahwa, why is kaffarah required for intentional eating in Ramadan?
A) Because the Quran explicitly mentions eating and kaffarah together.
B) Because the manat (complete violation of the fast) is linguistically understood and applies to eating as well as intercourse.
C) Because priority (اولوية) is established since eating is more harmful than intercourse.
D) Because the Shafi'i school also requires kaffarah for eating.

Key Takeaway
Dalala al-fahwa is a valid linguistic inference that extends a ruling when the underlying cause (manat) is clearly understood by language speakers, and the Hanafi school does not require the unspoken case to be more deserving.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Al-Iqtida': Definition and Rules of Clusive Signification
Defines iqtida' and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-iqtida', distinguish it from hadhf, and explain why the muqtada lacks generality according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Iqtida' is a meaning understood by necessity to correct speech; it is not a word and has no generality.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of iqtida' (clusive signification), a key tool in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Unlike explicit words or omitted words (hadhf), iqtida' operates out of necessity to make speech valid. Understanding iqtida' is crucial for interpreting commands and contracts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Define al-iqtida'
Identify the definition: it is the indication of the spoken word to a meaning necessary for the correctness of that speech. Note that this meaning is understood by necessity, not through an explicit or omitted word.
The necessity can be rational (for reports: truth) or legal (for commands: feasibility).
2
Distinguish iqtida' from hadhf
Contrast iqtida' with hadhf (ellipsis). In hadhf, a specific word is omitted but intended, and its meaning is conveyed by one of the four indications of speech. In iqtida', no word is omitted; a meaning is understood directly from necessity.
Example: 'Ask the village' is hadhf (meaning 'ask the people'), while the slave-freeing case involves iqtida'.
3
Understand the dropping of elements
Examine how iqtida' can cause the dropping of elements that legally can drop (like acceptance in a sale by mutual consent). Necessity allows dropping certain pillars or conditions, but only those that can be dropped by law.
For example, in a gift (hiba), possession (qabd) cannot drop because it is a condition that cannot be dropped by necessity.
4
Analyze the ruling on generality
Understand the Hanafi position that the muqtada (the understood meaning) has no generality (umum) or specificity (khushush). It is only estimated to the extent necessary for correction; it cannot be treated as a general statement then specified.
If a general meaning were needed for correctness (like 'I do not eat bread'), it is limited to that necessity. No takhsis can be applied because the muqtada is not the speaker's intended wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الاقتضاء، وهو دلالة المنطوق على ما يتوقف صحته عليه) وهي في الأخبار تكون بالصدق ( عقلاً أو شرعاً) واحترز بقوله دلالة المنطوق عن المقدر، فإن اللفظ المقدر هناك دال لا المنطوق المقتضي (فيعتبر هذا المعنى المدلول (مقدماً تصحيحاً للمقتضي) من الكلام لا بأن يقدر في نظم الكلام، بل يفهم المعنى فقط لهذه الضرورة (وهذا معنى قولهم : اللازم المتقدم اقتضاء بخلاف المتأخر فالمراد بالمتقدم ما يعتبر متقدماً لتصحيح الكلام، وهذا اصطلاح مغاير لما مر في فصل العام فإن ما مر كان متناولاً للمقدر في نظم الكلام (ويقدر) أي يعتبر (بقدره) أي ما تقتضيه الصحة لأنه ملحوظ ضرورة) فيتقدر بقدرها (فيسقط) منه إذا كان عقداً ما يحتمل السقوط) شرعاً من الأركان والشرائط، فإن الضرورة تسقط إياه، ولا يسقط ما لا يحتمل السقوط ومن ثمة استغنى البيع عن القبول) مع كونه ركناً فيه فيما إذا قال لسيد عبد أعتق عبدك عني بألف فقال : أعتقت عنك، فهذا الأمر لا يصح إلا إذا وقع البيع فاعتبر تصحيحاً لأمره، ولا حاجة فيه إلى القبول لأنه يسقط في التعاطي لوجود المراضاة، ويقع العتق عن الأمر ويكون الولاء له ويتأدى به الكفارة إن نوى، وعلى ذمته الألف الثمن، وفي هذا كله خلاف الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى وزفر (دون الهبة عن القبض) أي لا تستغني الهبة عن القبض، لأنه لا يحتمل السقوط أصلاً، فلو قال : أعتق عبدك عني ولم يقل بألف لا يصح هذا بأن يتقدمه هبة ولا يمكن اعتبارها للتصحيح لأنه لم يوجد القبض فيلغو الأمر، وإن أعتق لا يقع عن الأمر إلا عند أبي يوسف رحمه الله تعالى، فإنه يقول: الهبة الاقتضائية تسقط عنه القبض، وهذا تخصيص لنص اشتراط القبض من غير دليل مخصص فافهم ولا يعم هذا المقتضي ولا يخص لأنه زيادة أو نقصان أي لأن العموم زيادة، والخصوص نقصان لم يرد من العموم والخصوص أنه لا يقبل الاستغراق والتناول وعدمه لا ينكره عاقل كيف لو كان الضرورة إلى اعتبار معنى مستغرق تعين البتة، بل يراد بالعموم عموم يترتب عليه أحكامه من التخصيص والاستثناء، فلا يمكن ههنا أن يقال إن الكلام كان ظاهراً في العموم لكن خص منه البعض، فإن المقتضي ليس ملحوظاً للمتكلم وإنما يعتبر لتصحيح مراده، فيتقدر لضرورة التصحيح إن كان التصحيح باعتباره معنی مستغرق نحو لا آكل خبزاً، تعين وإلا لا كما في المثال المتقدم، ولا يصح اعتبار العام أولاً ثم التخصيص، لأنه إن كان المتوقف عليه أمراً عاماً فالتخصيص إفساد للكلام، وإن كان أمراً خاصاً فاعتبار العام من غير ضرورة، وهذا بخلاف الإشارة فإن المعنى هناك مدلول للكلام وهو ظاهر فيه، فيحتمل أن يخصص ويصرف عن الظاهر بمخصص، فقد وضح ما عليه الإمام فخر الإسلام أن المقتضى لا كما زعم بعض مشايخنا الكرام أن لا عموم للإشارة أيضاً له، والإشارة لها عموم فتأمل فيه وعند جمهور الحنفية المحذوف نحو واسأل القرية ليس منه) فإن المحذوف لفظ أراده المتكلم يدل على معناه بإحدى الدلالات الأربع والمقتضى معنى يفهم ضرورة تصحيح الكلام لا بتوسط اللفظ هذا هو الفرق العام، ثم لما كان بعض الصور التي اشتبهت على الخصم بالمقتضى مع كونها من المحذوف نحو اسأل القرية [يوسف: ۸۲] «والأعمال بالنيات» ورفع الله عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان فرقوا فرقاً آخر مختصاً بتلك الصور، أورده المصنف بقوله : ( والفرق أن في المحذوف) الذي يزعمونه مقتضى ينتقل حكم المذكور) من الإعراب بعد الاعتبار إليه فإنه لو قيل : اسأل أهل القرية يصير القرية مضافاً إليه، وكذا لو لا عموم
And among them is iqtida' (clusive signification), which is the indication of the spoken word to that upon which its correctness depends. And in reports (akhbar) it is with truth ( rationally or legally). By his statement 'indication of the spoken word' he excludes the estimated (al-muqaddar), for the estimated word there is indicative, not the spoken word that necessitates. So this understood meaning is considered prior to the correctness of the necessitating word, not by being estimated in the order of speech, but rather the meaning is understood only due to this necessity. And this is the meaning of their statement: 'The necessary implication that precedes is iqtida', as opposed to the one that follows.' So by 'preceding' is meant what is considered prior to correct the speech. And this terminology differs from what came in the chapter on generality, for that chapter dealt with the estimated in the order of speech. And it is estimated, i.e. considered, according to its measure, i.e. what correctness necessitates, because it is observed out of necessity. So it is estimated according to it. (So it drops) from it, if it is a contract, what legally can drop, from pillars and conditions, for necessity causes it to drop, and what cannot drop does not drop. Hence a sale becomes independent of acceptance (qabul), despite it being a pillar, in the case where he says to the owner of a slave: 'Free your slave for me for a thousand,' and he replies: 'I freed on your behalf.' This command is not valid unless a sale occurs, so it is considered to correct his command, and there is no need for acceptance because it drops in hand-to-hand transaction (al-ta'ati) due to mutual consent. The freeing occurs on behalf of the commander, his wala' is for him, and it fulfills the expiation (kaffara) if he intended, and the thousand price is on his liability. In all this, there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and Zufar. (But not the gift (hiba) regarding possession (qabd)), i.e. the gift is not independent of possession, because it cannot drop at all. So if he says: 'Free your slave on my behalf,' and does not say 'for a thousand,' this cannot be preceded by a gift, and it is not possible to consider it for correction because possession did not occur, so the command becomes void. And if he frees, it does not occur on behalf of the command except with Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, for he says: the iqtida' gift drops possession. And this is a specification of the text stipulating possession without a specifying evidence, so understand. And this iqtida' is neither general nor specific, because it is increase or decrease. i.e. because generality is increase and specificity is decrease. It is not meant that it does not accept encompassment and non-encompassment – no rational person denies that. Rather, what is meant by generality is a generality whose rulings (like specification and exception) follow; so here it cannot be said that the speech was apparent in generality but some was excluded from it, because the iqtida' is not intended by the speaker, it is only considered to correct his intention, so it is estimated according to the necessity of correction. If the correction requires a general meaning like 'I do not eat bread', then it is determined; otherwise no, as in the preceding example. And it is not valid to consider the general first then specify, because if what is needed is a general matter, specification would spoil the speech; if it is a specific matter, considering the general is without necessity. This is unlike ishara (indication), for the meaning there is a signification of the speech and is apparent in it, so it can be specified and diverted from the apparent by a specifier. So it is clear what Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) held: that al-muqtada does not [have generality], contrary to what some of our esteemed teachers claimed that ishara also has no generality, while ishara has generality, so ponder. And according to the majority of Hanafis, the omitted (al-mahdhuf) like 'Ask the village' is not from iqtida'. For the omitted is a word intended by the speaker, indicating its meaning through one of the four indications, while iqtida' is a meaning understood out of necessity to correct the speech, not through the medium of a word. This is the general distinction. Then, because some cases were ambiguous to the opponent as being iqtida' while they are from al-mahdhuf, like 'Ask the village' [Yusuf 82], 'Actions are by intentions', and 'Allah has lifted from my nation error and forgetfulness', they made another distinction specific to those cases, which the author presented by saying: (The distinction is that in al-mahdhuf) which they claim to be iqtida', the ruling of the mentioned word (in terms of i'rab) is transferred to it after consideration. For if it were said: 'Ask the people of the village', 'the village' becomes a possessive noun. And similarly if not general.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage defines iqtida' (clusive signification) as the indication of a spoken word to something that is necessary for the correctness of that speech. It is a rational or legal necessity: for reports, it must be true to be valid; for commands, it requires feasibility. The key point is that iqtida' is not a word that is estimated in the text; rather, a meaning is understood out of necessity to make the utterance valid. This contrasts with hadhf (ellipsis), where a specific word is omitted but intended by the speaker. The passage explains that iqtida' can cause the dropping of certain elements that can be dropped by law, like acceptance (qabul) in a sale by mutual consent (al-ta'ati) when someone says 'Free your slave for me for a thousand' and the owner says 'I freed on your behalf' – the sale is valid without an explicit acceptance because mutual consent makes it drop. However, a gift (hiba) cannot drop possession because possession is a condition that cannot be dropped by necessity. The passage also clarifies that the muqtada (the understood meaning) does not have generality (umum) or specificity (khushush) because it is not part of the speaker's intention; it is only considered for correction. Therefore, it cannot be said to be general and then specified. This is different from ishara (indication by the text) which has generality. Finally, the passage distinguishes iqtida' from hadhf: in hadhf, a word is omitted but its meaning is indicated by one of the four indications (dalalat al-alfaz al-arba'), while in iqtida', the meaning is understood directly from necessity without a word.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtidā’): The indication of a spoken word to a meaning that is necessary for the correctness of that speech, understood by necessity without being a word in the text
المقتضي (al-muqtaḍā): The meaning that is understood by necessity to correct the speech; it is not intended by the speaker but considered for correctness
المحذوف (al-mahdhūf): An omitted word that the speaker intended and whose meaning is conveyed by one of the four indications (dalalat al-alfaz al-arba')
التعاطي (al-ta'ātī): Hand-to-hand transaction without formal offer and acceptance, considered valid in Hanafi school

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about iqtida'?
A) Iqtida' has generality (umum) like explicit statements.
B) Iqtida' can cause the dropping of any element, including possession in a gift.
C) Iqtida' operates only in commands, not in reports.
D) Iqtida' is a meaning understood by necessity to correct the speech, and it lacks generality.

Key Takeaway
Iqtida' is a crucial tool in Hanafi usul: it allows a meaning necessary for correctness to be inferred without being stated, but it cannot be treated as having generality, and it only drops elements that can legally be dropped.
