المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية هذا الدليل مدلولاً للكلام (بل) هذا (بالعقل، وهو قول الحنفية أن العدم أصلي) أي ليس من وإن كان مثبتاً بدليل آخر (لا لغوي) مفهوم من هذا الكلام ولهذا لا ينسخ إن لم يجوز تأخير المخصص أو لا يخص إن جوز قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأُحِلَّ لَكُم مَّا وَرَاءَ ذَلِكُمْ ﴾ [النساء: ٢٤] بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنكُمْ طَوْلًا أَن يَنكِحَ المُحْصَنَاتِ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ فَمِن مَّا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُم مِّن فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ ﴾ [النساء : ٢٥] خلافاً للشافعي ومن تبعه فإن مفهومه وهو عدم جواز نكاح الإماء عند استطاعة الحرة، وعدم جواز نكاح الأمة الكتابية لما كان عنده حكماً شرعياً خص من عموم حل النساء، وأما عندنا فهو عدم أصلي بالنسبة إلى هذه الآية، أي إنها غير مثبتة لحلهما، فلا يصلح ناسخاً ولا مخصصاً فتدبر (و) قالوا :(ثانياً : قول يعلى بن أمية (العمر) أمير المؤمنين رضي الله عنهما ما بالنا نقصر وقد أمنا روى مسلم وأبو داود والترمذي والنسائي قال : قلت لعمر بن الخطاب : ليس عليكم جناح أن تقصروا من الصلاة إن خفتم أن يفتنكم الذين كفروا) فقد أمن الناس فقال : عجبت مما عجبت فسألت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : «صدقة تصدق الله بها عليكم فاقبلوا صدقته ففهم أمير المؤمنين انتفاء القصر عند انتفاء الخوف وهو مفهوم الشرط حتى عجب من بقاء القصر مع عدم الخوف وسأل (والجواب) عدم تسليم فهمه من اللفظ و(جواز بنائها أي بناء الصلاة في زعمه على الأصل وهو الإتمام لأن ما وراء الشرط مسكوت فيبقى على الأصل فإن قلت : قد روى البخاري عن أم المؤمنين عائشة رضي الله عنها فرض الله الصلاة حين فرضها ركعتين ركعتين في السفر والحضر، فأقرت صلاة السفر وزيد في صلاة الحضر، فالإتمام ليس أصلاً حتى يفهم من الأصل، قلنا: لو سلم أن مذهب أمير المؤمنين عمر ذلك وأغمض عن ظاهر الآية، فمعنى كلام أم المؤمنين أنه أقرت فيما شرع فيه القصر وكنى عنه بالسفر لأنه موضع القصر، ففهم أمير المؤمنين لعله لأن تقرير الركعتين معلق بالخوف ففيما وراءه يبقى على الحكم المتقرر بعد النسخ وهو الأربع فعجب فسأل، وبعضهم حملوا الآية على صلاة الخوف وعزي إلى ابن عباس لكن حديث يعلى يخالفه، وكذا رواية النسائي عن عبد الله بن خالد بن أبي أسيد أنه قال لابن عمر: كيف تقصر الصلاة وإنما قال عز وجل: فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن نَقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلَوةِ إِنْ خِفْتُمْ ﴾ [النساء: ١٠١] فقال ابن عمر: يا ابن أخي إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتانا ونحن ضلال فعلمنا، فكان فيما علمنا أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أمرنا أن نصلي في السفر ركعتين، فتدبر.
The third essay on linguistic principles: This proof is a meaning of speech (but) this (by reason, and it is the Hanafi statement that non-existence is original) i.e., it is not from, even if proven by another proof (not linguistic) understood from this speech, and because of this it is not abrogated if delaying the specifier is not allowed, nor does it specify if allowed. Allah's statement: "And lawful to you are what is beyond that" [4:24] with His statement: "And whoever among you cannot afford to marry the believing free women, then from what your right hands possess of your believing maidens" [4:25] contrary to al-Shafi'i and those who follow him, for its implication - which is the impermissibility of marrying slave women when free women are available, and the impermissibility of marrying a kitabi slave woman - since according to him it is a legal ruling, it specifies the generality of the permissibility of women. But according to us, it is original non-existence relative to this verse, i.e., it does not establish their permissibility, so it is not suitable as an abrogator nor a specifier. So reflect. (And) they said: (Secondly: the statement of Ya'la ibn Umayyah (to 'Umar) the Commander of the Faithful, may Allah be pleased with them both: "What is with us that we shorten while we are safe?" Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and al-Nasa'i narrated: He said: I said to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab: "There is no blame upon you if you shorten from the prayer if you fear that the disbelievers may harm you" but the people are safe. He said: I wondered at what you wondered, so I asked the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and he said: "A charity that Allah has given you, so accept His charity." So the Commander of the Faithful understood the negation of shortening upon the negation of fear, which is the implication of the condition, to the extent that he wondered at the continuation of shortening with the absence of fear and asked. (And the answer) is the non-acceptance of his understanding from the wording, and (the permissibility of building it, i.e., building the prayer in his view upon the original which is completion, because what is beyond the condition is left silent, so it remains upon the original. If you say: Al-Bukhari narrated from the Mother of the Believers 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her: Allah made the prayer obligatory when He prescribed it as two rak'ahs two rak'ahs in travel and residence, then the travel prayer was confirmed and the residence prayer was increased. So completion is not original such that it is understood from the original. We say: Even if we accept that the view of the Commander of the Faithful 'Umar was that, and he overlooked the apparent meaning of the verse, then the meaning of the Mother of the Believers' statement is that she affirmed what was legislated regarding shortening, and referred to it as travel because it is the place of shortening. So the Commander of the Faithful understood perhaps because the establishment of two rak'ahs is conditional upon fear, so beyond that remains upon the established ruling after abrogation, which is four, so he wondered and asked. And some carried the verse upon the prayer of fear, and it was attributed to Ibn 'Abbas, but the hadith of Ya'la contradicts it. Similarly, the narration of al-Nasa'i from 'Abdullah ibn Khalid ibn Abi Usayd that he said to Ibn 'Umar: How can you shorten the prayer when Allah Almighty said: "There is no blame upon you if you shorten from the prayer if you fear" [4:101]? Ibn 'Umar said: O my nephew, the Messenger of Allah came to us while we were astray and taught us. Among what he taught us was that the Messenger of Allah, his family and companions, peace be upon him, ordered us to pray two rak'ahs in travel. So reflect.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage from the third essay on linguistic principles presents the Hanafi view that original non-existence ('adam asli) is a rational principle, not a linguistic implication. The author begins by stating that the Hanafi doctrine is that 'adam asli is established by reason, not by language. This means that when no textual ruling exists, the default state is non-existence, and this default cannot be overridden by later texts through abrogation or specification, because it is not a ruling derived from speech. The author provides two proofs. First, a rational argument illustrated by the two verses from Surah Nisa. The general permission to marry women in verse 24 is, according to the Shafi'i school, specified by the condition in verse 25 (inability to marry free women) such that when the condition is absent, the prohibition of marrying slave women is understood as a legal ruling. For the Hanafis, however, this absence is not a ruling from the text but an original non-existence; the text simply does not establish permissibility in that case, so it cannot be used to specify or abrogate. The second proof is the hadith of Ya'la ibn Umayyah. 'Umar wondered why shortening (qasr) continues in safety when the Quranic permission is conditional on fear. The Prophet explained that shortening is a charity from Allah, not dependent on the condition. The Hanafi response is that 'Umar's understanding from the wording is not accepted; the silence beyond the condition leaves the original ruling (completion or the established practice) intact. The author then addresses a possible objection from a hadith of 'Aisha suggesting that the original prayer was two rak'ahs, explaining that this is abrogated and the travel prayer is a specific legislation. The passage concludes with Ibn 'Umar's reference to the sunna of traveling prayer, reinforcing the Hanafi position that the condition does not linguistically negate the ruling.

Key Terms
العدم الأصلي (al-'adam al-asli): The original state of non-existence that is a rational principle, not a linguistic implication from speech; it cannot be abrogated or specified.
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): The implication of a condition, i.e., that the ruling in the conditional clause applies only when the condition is met, and its opposite when not met, according to some schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why can the original non-existence ('adam asli) not be used to specify or abrogate a general text?
A) Because it is a linguistic implication that cannot be overridden.
B) Because it is a rational principle, not a ruling derived from speech.
C) Because it is established by consensus and cannot be changed.
D) Because it is specific to the verses of Surah Nisa.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi doctrine of al-'adam al-asli establishes that original non-existence is a rational default, not a linguistic ruling, and therefore cannot be used to specify or abrogate texts.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Conditional Clauses: Prevention vs. Delay
Dispute on whether conditional clauses create a cause immediately or only upon fulfillment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that a conditional clause prevents (man') the formation of a cause, distinguish it from the Shafi'i view of ta'khir, and apply the difference to cases of conditional divorce, vows, and expiation.

Core Idea
Hanafi: condition blocks cause; Shafi'i: cause exists, ruling delayed.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author explores a fundamental usul dispute: when a legal ruling is attached to a condition, does the condition prevent the cause from arising until fulfilled, or does it simply delay the ruling while the cause is already present? The answer affects many branches of fiqh, from divorce to oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Understand that the Hanafi school says the condition prevents (man') the formation of a cause (sabab), while the Shafi'i school says the cause is already formed but the ruling is delayed (ta'khir).
This is the central point of contention.
2
Analyze the proofs
Examine the Hanafi rational argument (the conditional clause alone has no effect, as seen in permissible attachment to impossibilities) and the Shafi'i linguistic argument (condition as adverb, so ruling is present now).
The different methods of argumentation reflect deeper usul principles.
3
Apply to branch cases
Work through the three derivatives: conditional divorce/manumission at ownership, premature vow fulfillment, and premature expiation of oath. See how each school rules differently.
These branches show the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
4
Connect to deeper usul
Recognize that this dispute is built upon whether contract formulas are performative (insha') or declarative (ikhbar). The Shafi'i view leans toward insha', making the formula an immediate cause; the Hanafi view leans toward ikhbar, so the cause is only realized upon the condition.
This linguistic-philosophical layer underlies the entire discussion.
5
Evaluate the thamarah
Identify the practical consequences: Hanafi rulings that wait for the condition are more cautious in preserving validity; Shafi'i rulings that assume cause now may lead to invalidity if the locus is not owned at the time of utterance.
The difference is not just theoretical but affects actual enforceability.

The third article on linguistic principles: Thus al-Shafi'i argued that attaching a ruling to a condition negates it before or after, on the basis that without it, it would exist. For example, a man says to his slave: 'You are free' — its import is the existence of freedom as an attribute of the slave. If he says, 'If you enter the house,' and attaches it, it necessitates annihilating it from its locus and negating the existence of his statement 'You are free.' So it is established that the attachment, just as it necessitates existence at the condition, necessitates negation before it. Then he said after explaining the derivative points of difference: 'As for the scholars of the Shafi'i school, they held that when causes necessitating rulings are attached to conditions, the attachment is a disposal in the causes by annihilating them, not in their rulings. And at the existence of the condition, the rulings begin to exist, just as at the existence of causes, there is no difference between them in the ruling of commencement.' So his statement 'on the basis that without it it would exist' meant that his statement that the condition indicates the negation of the ruling when it is absent is based on considering the condition like an exception, removing what is other than the estimated existence of the condition. And he indicated by his statement 'for they held, etc.' that they held that the condition, along with the conditional clause, conveys a restricted ruling, and the conditional clause alone has no import to serve as a cause before the existence of the condition. As for Imam Fakhr al-Islam, he first summarized completely and said: 'The gist of it is that the conditioned (mu'allaq) upon a condition, according to us, does not become a cause; rather, the condition prevents causation. Al-Shafi'i said: It is delayed.' Then after laying out the derivative points of difference, he indicated what we said by his statement in al-Shafi'i's argument: 'He said: Because obligation is established by the declaration of obligation were it not for the condition, so the condition becomes an annihilator of what would have existed without it, thus the condition is a delayer, not a preventer. That without the condition, the conditional clause would be a declaration of obligation — the establishment of the ruling on all assessments in language. So the condition excepts what is other than it and the absence of the ruling therein, so the condition itself is a delayer in language, not a preventer of speech.' And he only established the discussion in this which is among the particulars of the conditioned, i.e., when the conditional clause is a cause for another ruling legally, to mention some derivative branches as is their noble practice, and their intention is what we mentioned. Therefore, none of what was mentioned refutes it. This is the gist of the exposition, but upon reflection there remains a point to ponder, so ponder. And derived from it is the attachment of divorce and manumission to ownership: according to us, it occurs when ownership exists — according to us, due to its lack of causality at present, it becomes a cause at the existence of the condition, i.e., ownership, so it encounters a locus that is owned. According to him, it is not valid; rather, it is invalid because it becomes a cause at present and the locus is not owned, so it is futile, and nothing occurs at the existence of the condition. And derived from it is the premature fulfillment of a conditional vow, such as 'If my son comes, then I must give such-and-such charity.' According to us, since this vow does not become a cause for obligation except at the existence of the condition, premature fulfillment is not valid, being performance before the existence of obligation. According to him, since it became a cause at present, and the condition only prevented the obligation of performance due to their separation in financial matters, performance before the oath-breaking is valid. And derived from it also is the premature fulfillment of an oath's expiation if it is financial before oath-breaking. According to him, it is permissible because oath-breaking is a condition and the oath is a cause, and the cause has occurred, so it becomes obligatory in the dhimmah, and even if it is not obligatory to perform due to the mentioned separation, performance before oath-breaking is valid. According to us, it is not permissible because the cause of expiation according to us is oath-breaking, not the oath, so performance before oath-breaking is performance before the existence of the cause. And there is discussion: the derivation is obscure, for the discussion is about the grammatical condition (shart nahwi) whether it blocks causality or not, and oath-breaking is not a grammatical condition. And what was answered, that Allah's statement {So its expiation is feeding ten poor people} [al-Ma'idah 89] means 'If you break your oath, then its expiation is...' so it became attached by a condition, then does it prevent the causality of this speech for obligating expiation or only delay the ruling? There is manifest difficulty in this. Likewise, the answer that the one who swears says 'By Allah, I will do such-and-such' is in the strength of 'If I break my oath, then I must pay expiation.' Rather, the truth is that it is not a condition; it is only brought due to its similarity to the grammatical condition, and it is only derived from the fact that the oath is a cause for expiation, as he held, or oath-breaking, as we hold. So performing it before oath-breaking is performance after the establishment of the cause according to him, so it is permissible in financial matters as mentioned. According to us, it is not established as a cause, so it is not permissible. Understand. I say: The more appropriate view is that this issue of whether the condition prevents causality or ruling is built upon whether the formulas of contracts and dissolutions are performative (insha') or declarative (ikhbar) that imply the performative, which is the motivator of the ruling in reality, and they only imply the performative by virtue of being an account of it. So whoever holds the first view (like the Shafi'is) allows no attachment except considering the ruling existing due to the existence of the formula at present, which is apparent, and it is the cause for the existence of the contract. Whoever holds the second view (like our eminent scholars) holds that the cause exists with him, namely the performative that is the motivator, because it only proves implication by necessity of validating the declarative, and there is no implication in attachment for the cause, which is the performative, except at the existence of the condition, because attachment does not depend for its validity and truthfulness except on the existence of the necessary when the necessitating exists, no more. Do you not see that attachment is permissible in impossible things, even though no conditional clause exists at all? So ponder. And there is apparent consideration in it: The Shafi'is' going to the performative view does not benefit them, for the dispute remains after that, because the fact that the formula is a cause absolutely is not agreed upon; rather, its causality at present is only in immediate cases, whereas in attachment, the difference arises. According to us, there is no causality, contrary to them. The commentator said: It will come that these dissolutions and contracts, on the assumption that they are declaratives, are accounts of a divorce that the speaker considers when speaking, and with this considered divorce, the divorce is effective and the speaker, when speaking, considers the attachment of the divorce completely first, then speaks. So the reported thing is actualized at present: is it a cause or not? The Shafi'is may say that it has become a cause but the ruling is delayed due to attachment, so the Hanafis' going to the declarative view does not benefit them. Moreover, the difference is possible even on the assumption of the declarative view: whether they are declarations about effecting divorce at present such that it occurs when the condition is fulfilled, or about the divorce that occurs at that time. So the declarative view is not beneficial. Understand. And in al-Talwih and al-Tahrir, this issue, including the concept of the condition, is built upon a difference in the view of conditionality. The grammarians said: The ruling is in the conditional clause alone, and the condition restricts it, like an adverb and a circumstantial clause. So the meaning of 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' is: you are divorced at the time of your entering the house, the state being that you are entering the house. Al-Sayyid said in his glosses on Sharh al-Talkhis that no grammarian holds this view except the author of al-Miftah, apparently. Supporting this is what is in Daw' al-Misbah: the conditional particle removes the condition and the conditional clause from being a complete sentence and from being informative of silence. The rationalists said: The ruling is between them, i.e., a conditional ruling different from categorical statements. The condition and the conditional clause are two parts of the speech: one is the subject of that ruling, the other is the predicate. Al-Shafi'i held the first view attributed to the grammarians, and thus held that the cause is now established due to the existence of the ruling of divorce now, and its non-existence at non-existence means the non-existence of the ruling of the conditional clause at the non-existence of the condition is a legal ruling understood from the condition because the condition, being like an adverb and circumstantial clause, conveys the ruling of the conditional clause on the matter.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the legal effect of conditional clauses. The discussion centers on whether a conditional statement creates a cause (sabab) for a ruling immediately upon utterance or only upon fulfillment of the condition. The Shafi'i position, attributed to grammarians, is that the condition acts like an adverb or circumstantial clause, so the ruling is present in the conditional clause itself; the condition merely restricts the time or state. Thus, the cause is established now, but the ruling is delayed (mu'akhkhar). The Hanafi position, as represented by Imam Fakhr al-Islam, is that the condition prevents (man') the formation of a cause; the conditional statement does not become a cause until the condition is met. The text then applies this principle to three branches: (1) conditional divorce and manumission tied to ownership of the spouse/slave; according to Hanafis, the divorce or manumission occurs only when ownership exists, while according to Shafi'is, it is invalid because the cause was formed when the person was not owned. (2) Premature fulfillment of a conditional vow; Hanafis disallow it because the obligation does not exist until the condition, while Shafi'is allow it, analogizing to zakat before the year's end. (3) Premature payment of expiation for a conditional oath; Hanafis disallow because the cause is the oath-breaking itself, while Shafi'is allow as the oath creates the cause. The text then delves into a deeper usul discussion on whether contract formulas (sighat al-'uqud) are performative (insha') or declarative (ikhbar). The Shafi'is lean toward insha', meaning the formula itself is the cause. The Hanafis lean toward ikhbar, meaning the formula reports a mental act, which only becomes a cause upon condition. The author notes that this debate does not resolve the original dispute, as even if the formula is declarative, the reported divorce could be considered occurring now or later. Finally, the text mentions that grammarians and rationalists differ on the nature of conditional sentences: grammarians see the condition as a qualifier, so the ruling is in the conditional clause; rationalists see a conditional judgment distinct from categorical ones. Al-Shafi'i follows the grammarians, leading to his position.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition that is attached to a ruling; it either prevents the cause (Hanafi) or delays the ruling (Shafi'i).
سبب (sabab): The cause that necessitates a legal ruling; Hanafis say it does not arise until the condition is met; Shafi'is say it arises immediately but the ruling is delayed.
إنشاء (insha'): A performative utterance that itself creates the legal effect; Shafi'is consider contract formulas as insha'.
إخبار (ikhbar): A declarative utterance that reports a mental act; Hanafis consider contract formulas as ikhbar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a conditional clause (e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are divorced') before the condition is fulfilled?
A) The divorce is effective immediately, but its execution is delayed.
B) The clause is invalid and has no effect even after the condition is met.
C) The clause does not create a cause; it only becomes effective upon fulfillment of the condition.
D) The cause is created but the ruling is suspended until the condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a condition prevents the cause from arising, ensuring that the ruling is valid only when its requirements are met, thus avoiding premature or invalid effects.

The Core Dispute: Conditionality and Causality in Oaths and Contracts
Analyzing the core dispute on ta'liq and sababiyya with an oath example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a conditional clause creates an immediate legal cause, and apply this to the permissibility of pre-emptive expiation in oaths.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a conditional clause suspends the cause; in Shafi'i, it delays the effect but the cause is present from the moment of utterance.

This lesson explores a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: when a person says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' does the divorce take legal effect immediately upon utterance (suspended only in enforcement), or does the entire statement produce a suspensive ruling that only materialises upon the condition's occurrence? The practical consequence affects the permissibility of performing expiation (kaffarah) before the oath is breached. We will examine the arguments from both sides and the underlying linguistic and performative assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: ta'liq and sababiyya
Recognise that the core question is whether a conditional clause (shart) in a legal speech act creates an immediate cause for the consequence (jaza) or merely suspends the ruling until the condition occurs.
The issue is often discussed in the context of oaths and conditional divorces.
2
State the Hanafi position and proof
The Hanafi school holds that the entire conditional statement produces a suspensive (ta'liqi) ruling; the cause is not established until the condition is fulfilled. Hanafi proof: impossibility of some consequences proves that the consequence does not carry a pre-existing ruling; e.g., 'if you fly' is a valid conditional even though the consequence is impossible, showing it is not a standalone predicate.
This view is attributed to Abu Hanifa and the early Hanafi masters.
3
State the Shafi'i position and proof
The Shafi'i school, aligning with grammarians, holds that the ruling resides in the consequence alone, and the condition acts like an adverb or circumstantial phrase. Thus, the cause (the ruling of divorce) is present at the moment of utterance, but its effect is delayed by the condition acting as a preventer (mani').
Imam al-Shafi'i is the main proponent of this view.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf: expiation before breach
Practical consequence: If a person swears an oath to do something, then performs the expiation (kaffarah) before breaking the oath, is it valid? Hanafi: no, because the cause (the breach) has not occurred yet. Shafi'i: yes, because the cause (the oath itself) is already established; the condition only delays the obligation of expiation.
The passage mentions that this ruling is also affected by whether the contract formulas are insha' (performative) or ikhbar (informative).
5
Understand why the dispute remains even after the insha'/ikhbar discussion
The passage notes that even if one accepts the Shafi'i view that contract formulas are performative (insha'), the dispute about causality in conditional clauses persists, because the condition changes the nature of the cause itself. Similarly, the Hanafi adoption of the informative (ikhbar) view does not automatically resolve the dispute.
This shows the depth of the usul disagreement.

Does this prevent the causality of this speech for obligating the expiation, or merely delay the ruling? Therein is manifest absurdity. Similarly, the answer that the oath-taker's statement 'By Allah, I will do such and such' is in the strength of 'If I break the oath, then upon me is expiation'—but the truth is it is not a condition; rather it is brought due to its resemblance to a grammatical condition. Rather it is derived from the fact that the oath is a cause for expiation, as he (al-Shafi'i) holds, or the breaking of the oath is the cause as we (Hanafis) hold. So performing the expiation before the breaking is an action after the cause is established according to him, so it is not [valid], but it is permissible in financial matters as mentioned. According to us, it is said the cause is established, so it is not permissible. Understand. (I say: The more appropriate [view] is that this issue—whether the suspension [ta'liq] prevents causality or the ruling—is built upon whether the formulas of contracts and annulments are performative (insha') or informative (ikhbar) that necessitate the performative which is the cause of the ruling, in reality. The performative necessitates it only because it is a report of it. So whoever holds the first view (like the Shafi'is) has no suspension except with consideration of the existing ruling due to the existence of the formula at the moment, which is apparent, and it is the cause for the existence of the contract. Whoever holds the second (like our great shaykhs) says there is existence of the cause according to him, which is the performative that necessitates, because it is only established by necessity to correct the informative statement, and there is no necessity in suspension for the cause which is the performative except when the condition exists, because the validity and truth of the suspension only depend on the existence of the necessary when the necessary-condition exists, no more. Do you not see that suspension is allowed in impossible things while there is no existence of the consequence at all? Reflect. There is apparent objection in it: it does not benefit the Shafi'is to adopt insha', for the dispute remains, because the fact that the formula is an absolute cause may not be agreed upon. Rather its causality at the moment is only in immediate (munajjaz) cases; as for suspension, the disagreement arises. According to us, there is no causality, contrary to them. The 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' said: It will come that these annulments and contracts, assuming they are informative, are reports about a divorce that the speaker considers at the time of utterance. With this considered divorce, the speaker considers first the suspension of the absolutely binding divorce, then speaks. So the reported thing exists at the moment—is it a cause or not? The Shafi'is could say a cause has been established, but the ruling is delayed due to the suspension, so the Hanafis' adopting the informative does not benefit them. Moreover, disagreement is also possible on the assumption of informativity: are they reports about an immediate divorce that occurs when the suspended condition exists, or about the divorce that exists at that time? So the informative view does not help either. Understand. In al-Talwih and al-Tahrir: This, indeed the issue of mafhum al-shart (the implied meaning of the condition) also, is built upon a difference of opinion regarding the conditional statement. The grammarians said: the ruling is solely in the consequence (jaza'), and the condition is a restriction on it like an adverb or circumstantial phrase. So the meaning of 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' is: you are divorced at the time of entering the house, in the state that you are entering. Sayyid in the commentary on Talkhis said: No grammarian held this except the author of Miftah according to his apparent statement. Supported by what is in Daw' al-Misbah: the conditional particle removes the condition and consequence from [ordinary] speech and from informing about [an existing fact]. The logicians said: the ruling is between them, which is a suspensive ruling differing from categorical propositions. Both (the condition and consequence) are two parts of the speech, one is the subject of that ruling, and the other is the predicate. Imam al-Shafi'i inclined to the first view attributed to the grammarians, so he held that the cause is established now due to the existence of the ruling of divorce now, and non-existence at non-existence—i.e., the non-existence of the ruling of the consequence when the condition is absent—is a shar'i ruling understood (mafhum). Because since the condition is like a circumstantial phrase, the consequence imparts the ruling over all estimates, and the condition specifies it to some estimates and prevents from others. So the negation comes from the condition, so it becomes a ruling understood from it, and it also becomes shar'i because it is the import of the speech. Abu Hanifa inclined to the second view. So with the condition, he gave a suspensive ruling; thus the ruling of occurrence of the consequence does not exist from him, but rather it is realized when the condition exists, since he gave a suspensive ruling. So what remains beyond the suspended condition remains as it originally was. So here are two demands: The first: the derivation of mafhum al-shart is based on this difference. Its explanation: When al-Shafi'i inclined to the grammarians' view, the consequence according to him imparts the ruling over all estimates, and the condition specifies it, so negation is attributed to it. When Imam Abu Hanifa inclined to the logicians' view, the consequence according to him does not indicate any ruling at all; rather the whole [conditional statement] imparts a restricted ruling. So it does not indicate non-existence at non-existence; rather non-existence remains as it originally was. This is the summary of his statement, and there is discussion on it. First: If he intends by 'imparting the ruling of the consequence at the condition' that it imparts the establishment of the ruling in reality, but on the assumption of the existence of the condition in it, then for its truth, the consequence must be realized absolutely, and also the condition. This is corrupt, for the consequence may be impossible while the conditional statement is used conventionally and linguistically. Building speech on this falsehood is unfitting. How could such an imam, a genius with extensive knowledge, say this? If he intends by it imparting that the ruling of the consequence is established on the assumption of the existence of the condition in the manner of a hypothetical proposition, and its truth does not require the truth of the consequence in reality, but only on the assumption of the condition, this is equivalent to the conditional [view]. So what follows from the ruling being in the consequence is no different from what follows from the ruling being between them due to entailment. Understand. Second: We grant that, but we do not grant that the consequence on this assumption requires the ruling over all estimates and the condition specifies it. Rather the consequence is then restricted by the circumstance or adverb. When there is a restriction in the speech, it remains dependent on it, and from the whole a restricted ruling is derived. So non-existence at non-existence does not follow; rather it remains as it was, if one builds on the assumption that he is a proponent of the condition being a specifier of the consequence. This would only have a face if the consequence were restricted to the generality of estimates as we presented. But this does not suffice to prove that the consequence is a predicate and the condition is like a circumstance and adverb. Third: We grant that, but the disagreement remains; the Hanafis' adopting the logicians' view does not benefit them, because it is conceded that the whole imparts a suspensive ruling by explicit wording. Does it indicate in the silent part the non-existence of the consequence upon non-existence of the condition or not? One of the two is not necessarily determined, so the disagreement remains as it was. Understand. If you reflect, you will know that this is an objection to what we set forth from the foundation. Reflect. The second demand: the derivation of the issue of the establishment of causality is based on this difference. Its explanation: Since the whole of condition and consequence imparts a suspensive ruling, it does not necessitate the realization of the consequence, so causality is not established, as is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. As for him (al-Shafi'i), since the ruling is in the consequence, he imparts its establishment, except that the condition is a preventer, so it is established but for the preventer, which is the meaning of the establishment of causality. In this, al-Shafi'i is not benefited by adopting that, because the disagreement remains: the condition is a restriction that changes causality, so it does not remain a cause. As for the establishment of the ruling, adopting it does not benefit al-Shafi'i. Likewise in the Hashiya. Perhaps you will say: If the ruling is in the consequence, it is beneficial for the realization of its ruling in reality, except that the condition prevented it from immediate realization and restricted it.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a dense usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerning the nature of conditional clauses in legal speech—specifically whether a condition (shart) creates an immediate legal cause (sabab) or merely qualifies a pre-existing ruling. The author, commenting on a matn, expands the argument with multiple layers.

The issue arises in the context of oaths and contracts: when someone says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' does the divorce take effect as a cause immediately upon the utterance, or only when the condition occurs? The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa and the early Hanafi masters (mashayikhuna), is that the entire conditional statement produces a suspensive (ta'liqi) ruling. The condition does not create a present cause; rather, the ruling of divorce is suspended and only actualizes upon fulfillment of the condition. Consequently, expiation for a broken oath cannot be performed before the breach because the cause (the breach itself) has not yet occurred. In financial matters, however, early performance may be allowed as a distinct ruling.

In contrast, the Shafi'i view, following grammarians (ahl al-arabiyya), treats the condition like an adverb or circumstantial phrase. The consequence (jaza) alone carries the ruling, and the condition merely restricts it to a specific time or state. Thus, upon saying the conditional phrase, the ruling of divorce is already established as a cause—the condition acts as a preventer (mani') from immediate effect, but the cause itself is present. Hence, expiation before the breach is valid according to some Shafi'i formulations, because the cause (the oath) already exists.

The author then delves into the linguistic and logical foundations: whether contract formulas are performative (insha') or informative (ikhbar). Shafi'is generally see them as performative, creating an immediate effect; Hanafis view them as informative about a divorce that the speaker mentally intends, making the cause contingent on the condition. The debate also connects to the concept of mafhum al-mukhalafa: whether the non-occurrence of the condition implies the non-occurrence of the ruling. Shafi'is affirm this (hence the cause exists in the absence of the preventer); Hanafis deny it, arguing that the whole conditional statement restricts the ruling and no implication of negation arises outside the condition.

Throughout the commentary, the author critically examines both positions, noting weaknesses in the Shafi'i grammarians' analogy and defending the Hanafi logical approach, while also acknowledging that the dispute remains unresolved even if one adopts the other's premises. The passage concludes with the 'second demand' regarding causality (sababiyya) itself: Hanafis hold that causality is not established until the condition exists, while Shafi'is maintain it is established but suspended. The author stresses that even if one accepts the Shafi'i premise, the condition alters causality so fundamentally that the cause cannot be considered the same as in unconditional speech.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): The suspension of a legal effect (hukm) upon the occurrence of a future condition.
السببية (al-sababiyya): The quality of being a cause (sabab) for a legal ruling; whether the cause is established at the moment of utterance or only upon condition's fulfillment.
الإنشاء والإخبار (al-insha' wa al-ikhbar): Performative vs informative speech acts. Insha' creates a legal effect instantly; ikhbar reports a pre-existing state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the passage, when a person says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' does the divorce take legal effect immediately upon utterance?
A) Yes, because the formula is performative and creates the cause at once.
B) No, because the entire conditional statement gives a suspensive ruling, and the divorce only occurs upon entry.
C) Yes, but the effect is delayed until the condition occurs.
D) No, because divorce cannot be conditional at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view on conditionality (ta'liq) is that the cause is suspended until the condition occurs, leading to the impermissibility of pre-emptive expiation, in contrast to the Shafi'i view that the cause is present but delayed.

Linguistic Foundations: Mafhum al-Shart and the Grammarians vs Logicians
Analyzing the linguistic and logical foundations of conditional clauses and their impact on mafhum al-shart and sababiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the grammatical and logical views of conditional statements and derive the Hanafi position on mafhum al-shart and causality.

Core Idea
The disagreement over mafhum al-shart stems from whether the consequence alone carries the ruling (grammarians) or the whole conditional statement gives a suspensive ruling (logicians).

In this lesson, we delve into the linguistic and logical foundations that underlie the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute. The grammarians (ahl al-'arabiyya) held that the consequence (jaza) alone bears the ruling, with the condition acting as a restrictor. The logicians (ahl al-mizan) argued that the ruling is a suspensive one between the condition and consequence. This difference leads to opposing views on mafhum al-shart (the implied negation of the ruling when the condition is absent) and on whether the cause (sabab) is established at the moment of utterance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views: grammarians vs logicians on the locus of the ruling
The grammarians (ahl al-'arabiyya) hold that the ruling in a conditional statement resides solely in the consequence (jaza), and the condition is like a circumstantial phrase restricting it. The logicians (ahl al-mizan) hold that the ruling is a suspensive (ta'liqi) ruling that belongs to the entire conditional statement, distributed between condition and consequence.
This difference is crucial for mafhum al-mukhalafa.
2
Explain the grammatical view and its implication for mafhum al-shart
If the ruling is in the consequence alone, then the consequence applies to all estimates (taqadir) and the condition specifies it. Hence, when the condition is absent, the ruling is negated (mafhum al-mukhalafa). This makes mafhum al-shart a valid source of law according to al-Shafi'i.
Al-Shafi'i aligned with this grammatical view.
3
Explain the logical view and its implication for mafhum al-shart
If the ruling is suspensive, the conditional statement as a whole expresses a restricted ruling. There is no independent ruling in the consequence to be negated when the condition is absent. Therefore, mafhum al-shart is not a valid implication (according to Abu Hanifa).
Abu Hanifa aligned with the logicians' view.
4
Connect to the second demand: causality (sababiyya)
The same difference determines whether the cause is established immediately. For the grammarians, the cause (the consequence's ruling) is present but prevented; for the logicians, the cause is not established until the condition occurs. Thus, the dispute over mafhum directly impacts the issue of sababiyya.
The passage treats sababiyya as a derived consequence (المطلب الثاني).

Does this prevent the causality of this speech for obligating the expiation, or merely delay the ruling? Therein is manifest absurdity. Similarly, the answer that the oath-taker's statement 'By Allah, I will do such and such' is in the strength of 'If I break the oath, then upon me is expiation'—but the truth is it is not a condition; rather it is brought due to its resemblance to a grammatical condition. Rather it is derived from the fact that the oath is a cause for expiation, as he (al-Shafi'i) holds, or the breaking of the oath is the cause as we (Hanafis) hold. So performing the expiation before the breaking is an action after the cause is established according to him, so it is not [valid], but it is permissible in financial matters as mentioned. According to us, it is said the cause is established, so it is not permissible. Understand. (I say: The more appropriate [view] is that this issue—whether the suspension [ta'liq] prevents causality or the ruling—is built upon whether the formulas of contracts and annulments are performative (insha') or informative (ikhbar) that necessitate the performative which is the cause of the ruling, in reality. The performative necessitates it only because it is a report of it. So whoever holds the first view (like the Shafi'is) has no suspension except with consideration of the existing ruling due to the existence of the formula at the moment, which is apparent, and it is the cause for the existence of the contract. Whoever holds the second (like our great shaykhs) says there is existence of the cause according to him, which is the performative that necessitates, because it is only established by necessity to correct the informative statement, and there is no necessity in suspension for the cause which is the performative except when the condition exists, because the validity and truth of the suspension only depend on the existence of the necessary when the necessary-condition exists, no more. Do you not see that suspension is allowed in impossible things while there is no existence of the consequence at all? Reflect. There is apparent objection in it: it does not benefit the Shafi'is to adopt insha', for the dispute remains, because the fact that the formula is an absolute cause may not be agreed upon. Rather its causality at the moment is only in immediate (munajjaz) cases; as for suspension, the disagreement arises. According to us, there is no causality, contrary to them. The 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' said: It will come that these annulments and contracts, assuming they are informative, are reports about a divorce that the speaker considers at the time of utterance. With this considered divorce, the speaker considers first the suspension of the absolutely binding divorce, then speaks. So the reported thing exists at the moment—is it a cause or not? The Shafi'is could say a cause has been established, but the ruling is delayed due to the suspension, so the Hanafis' adopting the informative does not benefit them. Moreover, disagreement is also possible on the assumption of informativity: are they reports about an immediate divorce that occurs when the suspended condition exists, or about the divorce that exists at that time? So the informative view does not help either. Understand. In al-Talwih and al-Tahrir: This, indeed the issue of mafhum al-shart (the implied meaning of the condition) also, is built upon a difference of opinion regarding the conditional statement. The grammarians said: the ruling is solely in the consequence (jaza'), and the condition is a restriction on it like an adverb or circumstantial phrase. So the meaning of 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' is: you are divorced at the time of entering the house, in the state that you are entering. Sayyid in the commentary on Talkhis said: No grammarian held this except the author of Miftah according to his apparent statement. Supported by what is in Daw' al-Misbah: the conditional particle removes the condition and consequence from [ordinary] speech and from informing about [an existing fact]. The logicians said: the ruling is between them, which is a suspensive ruling differing from categorical propositions. Both (the condition and consequence) are two parts of the speech, one is the subject of that ruling, and the other is the predicate. Imam al-Shafi'i inclined to the first view attributed to the grammarians, so he held that the cause is established now due to the existence of the ruling of divorce now, and non-existence at non-existence—i.e., the non-existence of the ruling of the consequence when the condition is absent—is a shar'i ruling understood (mafhum). Because since the condition is like a circumstantial phrase, the consequence imparts the ruling over all estimates, and the condition specifies it to some estimates and prevents from others. So the negation comes from the condition, so it becomes a ruling understood from it, and it also becomes shar'i because it is the import of the speech. Abu Hanifa inclined to the second view. So with the condition, he gave a suspensive ruling; thus the ruling of occurrence of the consequence does not exist from him, but rather it is realized when the condition exists, since he gave a suspensive ruling. So what remains beyond the suspended condition remains as it originally was. So here are two demands: The first: the derivation of mafhum al-shart is based on this difference. Its explanation: When al-Shafi'i inclined to the grammarians' view, the consequence according to him imparts the ruling over all estimates, and the condition specifies it, so negation is attributed to it. When Imam Abu Hanifa inclined to the logicians' view, the consequence according to him does not indicate any ruling at all; rather the whole [conditional statement] imparts a restricted ruling. So it does not indicate non-existence at non-existence; rather non-existence remains as it originally was. This is the summary of his statement, and there is discussion on it. First: If he intends by 'imparting the ruling of the consequence at the condition' that it imparts the establishment of the ruling in reality, but on the assumption of the existence of the condition in it, then for its truth, the consequence must be realized absolutely, and also the condition. This is corrupt, for the consequence may be impossible while the conditional statement is used conventionally and linguistically. Building speech on this falsehood is unfitting. How could such an imam, a genius with extensive knowledge, say this? If he intends by it imparting that the ruling of the consequence is established on the assumption of the existence of the condition in the manner of a hypothetical proposition, and its truth does not require the truth of the consequence in reality, but only on the assumption of the condition, this is equivalent to the conditional [view]. So what follows from the ruling being in the consequence is no different from what follows from the ruling being between them due to entailment. Understand. Second: We grant that, but we do not grant that the consequence on this assumption requires the ruling over all estimates and the condition specifies it. Rather the consequence is then restricted by the circumstance or adverb. When there is a restriction in the speech, it remains dependent on it, and from the whole a restricted ruling is derived. So non-existence at non-existence does not follow; rather it remains as it was, if one builds on the assumption that he is a proponent of the condition being a specifier of the consequence. This would only have a face if the consequence were restricted to the generality of estimates as we presented. But this does not suffice to prove that the consequence is a predicate and the condition is like a circumstance and adverb. Third: We grant that, but the disagreement remains; the Hanafis' adopting the logicians' view does not benefit them, because it is conceded that the whole imparts a suspensive ruling by explicit wording. Does it indicate in the silent part the non-existence of the consequence upon non-existence of the condition or not? One of the two is not necessarily determined, so the disagreement remains as it was. Understand. If you reflect, you will know that this is an objection to what we set forth from the foundation. Reflect. The second demand: the derivation of the issue of the establishment of causality is based on this difference. Its explanation: Since the whole of condition and consequence imparts a suspensive ruling, it does not necessitate the realization of the consequence, so causality is not established, as is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. As for him (al-Shafi'i), since the ruling is in the consequence, he imparts its establishment, except that the condition is a preventer, so it is established but for the preventer, which is the meaning of the establishment of causality. In this, al-Shafi'i is not benefited by adopting that, because the disagreement remains: the condition is a restriction that changes causality, so it does not remain a cause. As for the establishment of the ruling, adopting it does not benefit al-Shafi'i. Likewise in the Hashiya. Perhaps you will say: If the ruling is in the consequence, it is beneficial for the realization of its ruling in reality, except that the condition prevented it from immediate realization and restricted it.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a dense usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerning the nature of conditional clauses in legal speech—specifically whether a condition (shart) creates an immediate legal cause (sabab) or merely qualifies a pre-existing ruling. The author, commenting on a matn, expands the argument with multiple layers.

The issue arises in the context of oaths and contracts: when someone says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' does the divorce take effect as a cause immediately upon the utterance, or only when the condition occurs? The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa and the early Hanafi masters (mashayikhuna), is that the entire conditional statement produces a suspensive (ta'liqi) ruling. The condition does not create a present cause; rather, the ruling of divorce is suspended and only actualizes upon fulfillment of the condition. Consequently, expiation for a broken oath cannot be performed before the breach because the cause (the breach itself) has not yet occurred. In financial matters, however, early performance may be allowed as a distinct ruling.

In contrast, the Shafi'i view, following grammarians (ahl al-arabiyya), treats the condition like an adverb or circumstantial phrase. The consequence (jaza) alone carries the ruling, and the condition merely restricts it to a specific time or state. Thus, upon saying the conditional phrase, the ruling of divorce is already established as a cause—the condition acts as a preventer (mani') from immediate effect, but the cause itself is present. Hence, expiation before the breach is valid according to some Shafi'i formulations, because the cause (the oath) already exists.

The author then delves into the linguistic and logical foundations: whether contract formulas are performative (insha') or informative (ikhbar). Shafi'is generally see them as performative, creating an immediate effect; Hanafis view them as informative about a divorce that the speaker mentally intends, making the cause contingent on the condition. The debate also connects to the concept of mafhum al-mukhalafa: whether the non-occurrence of the condition implies the non-occurrence of the ruling. Shafi'is affirm this (hence the cause exists in the absence of the preventer); Hanafis deny it, arguing that the whole conditional statement restricts the ruling and no implication of negation arises outside the condition.

Throughout the commentary, the author critically examines both positions, noting weaknesses in the Shafi'i grammarians' analogy and defending the Hanafi logical approach, while also acknowledging that the dispute remains unresolved even if one adopts the other's premises. The passage concludes with the 'second demand' regarding causality (sababiyya) itself: Hanafis hold that causality is not established until the condition exists, while Shafi'is maintain it is established but suspended. The author stresses that even if one accepts the Shafi'i premise, the condition alters causality so fundamentally that the cause cannot be considered the same as in unconditional speech.

Key Terms
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): The implied ruling that the absence of the condition negates the consequence's ruling (mafhum al-mukhalafa).
أهل العربية (ahl al-'arabiyya): The grammarians, who hold that the ruling in a conditional statement resides solely in the consequence.
أهل الميزان (ahl al-mizan): The logicians, who hold that the ruling is suspensive (ta'liqi) and resides in the entire conditional statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's view (the logical view), what is the implication of the absence of the condition in a conditional statement?
A) The absence implies the negation of the consequence's ruling.
B) The absence implies nothing; the matter remains as originally.
C) The absence creates a presumption of the consequence.
D) The absence makes the statement void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view that the entire conditional statement gives a suspensive ruling leads to the invalidity of mafhum al-shart as a source of law, and consequently to the non-establishment of the cause until the condition is fulfilled.

Mafhum al-`Adad: The Hanafi Dispute
Defining mafhum al-`adad and the Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mafhum al-`adad and explain the two Hanafi positions on its legal validity, citing the evidence presented in the text.

Core Idea
The mention of a specific number does not automatically negate the ruling for other numbers—Hanafis disagree on whether such negation is legally binding.

In this passage, the author defines mafhum al-`adad—the implied negation for numbers other than the one specified—and presents the internal Hanafi dispute. Some Hanafi scholars reject it as an independent concept, arguing that the default principle is permissibility unless there is evidence; others accept it as a valid indicator. We will dissect the definition, the evidence, and the reasoning of both sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
State the definition of mafhum al-`adad as given in the text: it is the negation of the ruling for any number different from the specified one.
The Arabic term is مفهوم العدد.
2
Locate the example
Find the Quranic example used (Surah al-Nur 24:4, 'flog them with eighty lashes') and explain how it illustrates the concept: it is understood that more than eighty is not prescribed.
The example shows the practical implication of accepting mafhum al-`adad.
3
Map the Hanafi disagreement
Identify the two groups among Hanafis: (1) rejecters, such as al-Bazdawi and al-Sarakhsi, and (2) exponents, such as al-Tahawi and Abu Bakr al-Razi.
Note that the text also mentions Shafi'i scholars who also reject it, but the focus is on Hanafis.
4
Analyze the evidence for rejection
Examine the argument: the default principle is that causing pain to a Muslim is impermissible without evidence. The hadith of five harmful animals is used to show that the number five is not exclusive—similar animals (like the wolf) share the ruling, proving that the number does not negate the ruling for other numbers.
The rejecters rely on the general principle and the implication from the hadith that the ruling is based on the attribute, not the number.
5
Evaluate the evidence for acceptance
Consider the position of the exponents: they hold that specification by number indicates that the ruling for other numbers is opposite. The text mentions a refutation in al-Hidayah against al-Shafi'i's use of analogy, but the author questions its completeness.
The exponents argue that the number itself is meaningful and exclusive, unless there is a separate indicator to the contrary.

And among them is the concept of number (mafhum al-`adad), which is the negation of the ruling established for a specific number from what is in excess of it. Like His statement (Exalted): 'And those who accuse chaste women and then do not bring four witnesses, flog them with eighty lashes' (Sura al-Nur:4). (Like the wolf) so it is known that the ruling for what is like it is the same, not different. This support is only valid if the wolf is not included in the biting dog. And it was said: The refutation is incomplete because it is established by the indication (dalil) of the text, not by analogy; and what is established by indication is not an increase. Also, if it were by analogy, it would override the concept. So reflect.

Commentary
This passage defines mafhum al-`adad: the implication that the ruling for the specified number does not apply to a greater or lesser number. The example from Quran (80 lashes for false accusation) shows that flogging is limited to 80; it is understood that more than 80 is not prescribed. Hanafis are divided: some reject it (like al-Bazdawi and al-Sarakhsi) as an independent concept, arguing that the negation of increase is simply due to lack of evidence and the default principle of not harming Muslims without right. They support this with the hadith of five harmful animals which the pilgrim may kill; the inclusion of the wolf by some scholars shows that the number five is not exclusive—the ruling applies to similar animals. Others, like al-Tahawi and Abu Bakr al-Razi, accept mafhum al-`adad as indicating that the ruling for other numbers is different. The author notes a refutation in al-Hidayah against al-Shafi'i's analogy, but then questions its completeness. The passage illustrates the internal Hanafi debate on whether a specified number negates the ruling for other numbers.

Key Terms
مفهوم العدد (mafhum al-`adad): The implied meaning that the ruling is negated for any number other than the one specified.
المخصوص بالعدد (al-makhsus bi-l-`adad): Something specified by a number, i.e., a text that mentions a particular quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The hadith explicitly says 'five and no more'.
D) The principle that numbers are always exclusive.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum al-`adad is a contested concept among Hanafis: some reject its legal force, others accept it as an indicator that the ruling is exclusive to the specified number.

Mafhum al-Laqab: The Implied Meaning of Proper Nouns
Defining mafhum al-laqab and the majority Hanafi rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mafhum al-laqab in a statement and explain the Hanafi rejection with reference to the absurd consequences and redundancy arguments.

Core Idea
Hanafi usul rejects mafhum al-laqab because it leads to absurdities and undermines explicit speech.

In Hanafi usul, not every implied meaning (mafhum) is accepted. One of the most controversial is mafhum al-laqab—the idea that a proper noun or common noun used in a ruling implies the opposite for everything else. The majority of Hanafis firmly reject this concept, and this lesson unpacks their two key arguments: that it disrupts the purpose of explicit speech and leads to self-evident falsehoods.

Guided Path
1
Define mafhum al-laqab
Understand the term: mafhum al-laqab is the implied meaning that the ruling contrary to the explicit text applies to anything not mentioned by the proper noun (or common noun).
Example: 'Zayd is standing' would imply that no one else is standing.
2
Identify the rejection by the Hanafi majority
Note that the majority of Hanafis, along with many other scholars, reject mafhum al-laqab. Some Hanbalis, Shafi'is (e.g., al-Daqqaq), and Malikis (e.g., al-Mandad) accept it.
3
Analyze the first argument: redundancy of explicit speech
Hanafis argue that if mafhum al-laqab were valid, the explicit text would become redundant because the concept would do all the work. This contradicts the purpose of clear speech.
This mirrors arguments used to reject mafhum al-sifa and al-shart.
4
Analyze the second argument: absurd consequences
If mafhum al-laqab is accepted, saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would imply that no one else is a messenger, which is kufr. Similarly, 'Zayd exists' would imply nothing else exists.
These consequences are clearly false and violate decisive texts.
5
Evaluate the counter-argument and note the Hanafi response
Some argue that these absurdities only appear when contradicting decisive texts (muhkamat), but the Hanafi response is that the argument stands as a dialectical point and the absurdity is inherent.
The passage notes that at the time of revelation to the polytheists, the decisive texts were not yet present, making the issue acute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And among them is the concept of the proper noun (mafhum al-laqab), which is the establishment of a ruling contrary to the explicit text beyond the proper noun (and what is intended includes the common noun). It is held by some Hanbalis and al-Daqqaq from the Shafi'is and al-Mandad from the Malikis. The majority of Hanafis and others are rejecters of it. For the majority, first: it is a necessary way to express the subject of the ruling by the explicit text, because without it the explicit text would be disrupted, and it is one of the greatest benefits and necessary in every speech. And one of the conditions of the concept is the negation of (such) benefit, and this applies exactly to the adjective and condition as you know. And for the majority, secondly: the necessary consequence of disbelief for one who says 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' (peace and blessings be upon him, his family, and his companions) on the assumption of the validity of mafhum al-laqab; because its concept is that no one else is the Messenger of Allah, and he is the Messenger of Allah, and (denying others) is disbelief. And the necessary consequence of disbelief for one who says 'Zayd exists' (apparently) because its concept is that no one else exists. The meaning is that disbelief according to the apparent wording. It was said: This argument (ilzam) was presented to al-Daqqaq in Baghdad. And there is room for debate: the concept is probabilistic (zanni) and weaker than the explicit text, especially the decisive (muhkam) texts, so it collapses when opposed by explicit texts. The decisive texts indicate the prophethood of all other messengers (peace be upon them), and the categorical (qati') proofs indicate the existence of others besides Allah. This is merely a dialectical argument because it would require disbelief regardless of opposition by other evidence, and this is extremely heinous. Also, this statement was addressed to the polytheists first and they were commanded to believe in it, and at that time the decisive texts were not present; then it would be...

Commentary
This passage defines mafhum al-laqab: the implied meaning that the ruling opposite to the explicit text applies to anything not mentioned by the proper noun (or common noun). For example, if the text says 'Zayd is standing', the mafhum would be that no one else is standing. The majority of Hanafis reject this concept entirely. The text gives two main arguments: (1) If we accept mafhum al-laqab, then every explicit statement would be unnecessary because the concept would do the work, disrupting the purpose of speech. This is the same reasoning used to reject mafhum al-sifa and al-shart. (2) Accepting it leads to absurdities: if someone says 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', the mafhum would be that no other messenger exists, which is disbelief (kufr) because it denies other prophets. Similarly, saying 'Zayd exists' would imply nothing else exists, which is false. The author notes that some argue these absurdities are only apparent when contradicting decisive texts, but even as a dialectical argument it is valid. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing that at the time of the Prophet, addressing polytheists, this argument would have serious implications. This passage shows the strong Hanafi rejection of mafhum al-laqab.

Key Terms
مفهوم اللقب (mafhum al-laqab): The implied meaning that the ruling contrary to the explicit text applies to anything not mentioned by the proper noun or common noun.
اللقب (al-laqab): A proper noun (like 'Zayd') or a common noun (like 'man') used as a name for the subject.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum al-laqab is rejected by the majority of Hanafis because it leads to absurd consequences and undermines the purpose of explicit speech.

The Criterion of Restriction (Hasr): Huwa Huwa vs. Common Predication
Analyzing how definite and indefinite predicates indicate restriction in Arabic syntax

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic structure that indicates restriction (hasr) in Arabic predication according to Hanafi usul, and apply the distinction between 'huwa huwa' and common predication to determine whether a given predicate implies exclusivity.

Core Idea
Only the 'huwa huwa' predication truly indicates restriction; a definite predicate alone does not.

In Hanafi usul, the interpretation of Arabic predicate structures is critical for deriving legal rulings from texts. This passage tackles a common confusion: does a definite predicate ('al-ma'rifa') imply exclusivity? The author clarifies that true restriction (hasr) is achieved only through a special structure called 'haml huwa huwa' (identity predication). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting divine address.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Recognize the two positions: one holds that a definite predicate indicates restriction while the indefinite does not; the other (author's view) rejects that definiteness alone yields restriction.
The dispute is recorded from Sharh al-Mukhtasar.
2
Distinguish the two types of predication
Differentiate between 'haml huwa huwa' (predication of identity) and 'al-haml al-shai'i' (common predication). In the former, the predicate is identical in essence to the subject; in the latter, the predicate is a concept applicable to many.
'Zaid is the standing one' could be identity if Zaid is the only one standing.
3
Apply the criterion of restriction
According to the author, restriction occurs only when the predicate is in 'huwa huwa' form. A definite predicate alone does not achieve restriction because the attribute can still be true of others.
For example, 'The standing one is Zaid' does not negate that Amr might also be standing.
4
Understand the rebuttal and verification
The author addresses the objection that definite predicates refer to essence, not concept. He clarifies that in customary predication, the predicate is the concept. The verification is that the manat (criterion) of hasr is 'huwa huwa', not definiteness.
Refer to his commentary on al-Sullam for fuller treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه ذاتاً موصوفة به وهو عارض (للأول ولا ينافي تحققه في غيره، فلا يفيد الحصر فافترقا كذا في شرح المختصر، ورد بأن الفرق المذكور (إنما هو في النكرة) الواقعة خبراً (دون المعرفة قيل في جواب الرد (قد تقرر ) في غير هذا الفن (أن) المحمول هو المفهوم دون الذات، سواء كان معرفة أو نكرة، أقول : التحقيق ههنا أن مناط الحصر) فيه (هو حمل هو هو) أي الأولى (لا الشائع إذ محصله ثبوت شيء للموضوع ولا ينافي الثبوت للغير (والنكرة) الواقعة خبراً (ظاهرة في الثاني فلا تفيد الحصر (والمعرفة) الواقعة خبراً ظاهرة (في الأول) فالمراد بها الذات الموصوفة، سواء وقع مسنداً إليه أو مسنداً وهذا لا ينافي ما تقرر) فإن ما تقرر أن المحمول بالحمل المتعارف هو المفهوم لا في الحمل الأولى على أن الحق هو الحكم على الطبيعة من حيث الانطباق على الذات دون الذات وقد حقق في السلم، ونحن أيضاً فصلنا القول في شرحه، ثم المقصود منه الاعتراض على هذا القائل وإن كان لا ينفع في هذا المقام (ثم إفادة تقديم ما حقه التأخير للحصر نحو : إِيَّاكَ نَعْبُدُ [الفاتحة: ٤] (وتفصيل أنواعها مع ما فيها من الاختلاف فمذكورة في علم المعاني فلا نذكره .
It being an essence described by it, and it is an accident (for the first) and does not negate its realization in others, so it does not benefit restriction, and they differed. Thus in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. And it was answered that the mentioned difference (is only with the indefinite) occurring as a predicate (not the definite. It was said in response to the reply: It is established in other than this art (that) the predicate is the concept, not the essence, whether it is definite or indefinite. I say: The verification here is that the criterion of restriction) in it (is the 'huwa huwa' predicate) i.e., the primary predicate (not the common one, because its sum is the affirmation of something for the subject and it does not negate the affirmation for others. (And the indefinite) occurring as a predicate (is apparent in the second, so it does not benefit restriction. (And the definite) occurring as a predicate is apparent (in the first). So what is meant by it is the essence described, whether it occurs as subject or predicate, and this does not contradict what is established) because what is established is that the predicate in the customary predication is the concept, not in the primary predication. The truth is that the judgment is upon the essence from the aspect of its applicability to the essence, not the essence itself. This has been verified in al-Sullam, and we have also detailed the statement in its commentary. Then the purpose of it is to object to this proponent, even if it does not benefit in this context. (Then the benefit of advancing what deserves delay for restriction, such as إِيَّاكَ نَعْبُدُ [al-Fatiha:4]. (And the details of its types with the disagreements therein are mentioned in the science of rhetoric, so we do not mention them.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the linguistic concept of hasr (restriction) in the context of Arabic predicates. The author begins by referencing a previous statement about an attribute being accidental and not negating its existence in others, hence not signifying restriction—this is from Sharh al-Mukhtasar. He then records a rebuttal: the difference between indefinite and definite predicates is that indefinite predicates do not indicate restriction while definite ones do. In reply, the author clarifies that the predicate is the concept (concept) not the essence itself, whether definite or indefinite. But he then gives his own verification: the true basis of restriction is the 'huwa huwa' predication (predicating the same term as both subject and predicate, like 'Zaid is Zaid' or 'Zaid is the standing one' in the sense of identity). The 'huwa huwa' structure implies that the attribute belongs exclusively to the subject and cannot apply to others. In contrast, a definite predicate alone (e.g., 'Zaid is the standing one') is ambiguous: it may mean the standing one is Zaid but does not negate that someone else might also be standing. An indefinite predicate, being indefinite, is even less restrictive. The author then addresses the objection that definite predicates are for the essence (the actual entity) and thus indicate restriction. He says that what is meant by definite is the essence described, but the predication is about the concept, not the essence itself in the primary sense. He refers to his own commentary on al-Sullam for further details. The purpose of this discussion is to refute an earlier proponent who claimed that definite predicates indicate restriction. Finally, he notes that other forms of restriction, such as advancing what normally comes later (like 'Iyyaka na'budu' where the object is fronted), are dealt with in the science of rhetoric. This passage exemplifies the rigorous linguistic analysis used in Hanafi usul to derive rules from Arabic syntax.

Key Terms
الحصر (al-ḥaṣr): Restriction or exclusivity in meaning; that an attribute applies only to a specific subject and to no other.
النكرة (al-nakira): An indefinite noun; when used as a predicate it indicates generality and does not imply restriction.
المعرفة (al-ma‘rifa): A definite noun; when used as a predicate, it may imply a specific essence but not necessarily restriction unless in a 'huwa huwa' structure.
حمل هو هو (ḥaml huwa huwa): A type of predication where the predicate is the same essence as the subject (e.g., 'Zaid is the standing one' implying Zaid alone is standing).
الحمل الشائع (al-ḥaml al-shā‘i‘): Common predication where the predicate is not identical to the subject; allows for the attribute to apply to others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which structure is the true indicator of restriction (hasr)?
A) Definite predicate alone
B) Indefinite predicate
C) The 'huwa huwa' predicate (definite subject and predicate referring to the same essence)
D) Fronting the object like 'إياك نعبد'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the 'huwa huwa' identity predication is the sole criterion for linguistic restriction (hasr) within predicate structures; a definite predicate alone is insufficient.
